Qisas for Killing Two Persons: One Execution Satisfies Both
Ruling on qisas for one killer of two, with a qiyas to shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for a single killer of two persons and explain the qiyas with shuf'a that justifies the present heir's right to execute qisas immediately.

Core Idea
One killer of two owes one death — each victim's right is fully satisfied because both rights aim at non-existence of life.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a specific case in qisas: one person kills two. The Hanafi ruling is that the killer is executed once, and that single execution satisfies both victims' rights fully. No blood money is owed. A further question arises when one victim's heir is present and the other is absent: the present heir may execute qisas immediately. The commentator draws an analogy with the law of pre-emption (shuf'a) to justify this priority. Understanding this ruling requires grasping how Hanafi jurisprudence resolves competing claims on a single object of retaliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act described? One person kills two people. The ruling is that the killer is subjected to qisas (retaliatory execution) for both, and no blood money is due. Note the hukm level: qisas is wajib.
The ruling applies when the killing is deliberate and the heirs of both victims are entitled to qisas.
2
Understand the rationale: full satisfaction of each right
Why does one execution satisfy both victims? Because each victim's right is the non-existence of the killer's life. One death achieves that non-existence for both. Thus each heir's right is fully realized by the same act.
This is a conceptual point: the right is not a share of the killer but rather the state of his death.
3
Analyze the sub-case: present vs. absent heir
If one heir is present and the other absent, the present heir may execute qisas immediately. Why? Because his right is established before the judge, while the absent heir's right is not yet presented. There is no competition (muzahamah) because the absent heir's right has not been claimed.
The present heir takes the full execution; the absent heir's right is delayed until he appears.
4
Examine the analogy with shuf'a (pre-emption)
The commentator likens this to co-pre-emptors in shuf'a: if one co-pre-emptor is present and the other absent, the present one can claim the entire property. Similarly, the present heir can execute full qisas because the absent heir's right is not yet manifested.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) that uses a well-known rule from property law to resolve a criminal procedure issue.
5
Apply the Hanafi principle
The underlying principle is that when multiple claimants have a right in the same object (here, the killer's life), and one's claim is established while the other's is not yet known, the established claim takes priority to avoid stalemate. This reflects the Hanafi preference for pragmatic resolution.
Note that this ruling might differ in other schools that require consent of all heirs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قتلت عدوي يقتل. وفي المحيط : وإذا قتل واحد رجلين يقتص بهما ولا يغرم الدية لأن بقتله صار كل واحد منهما مستوفياً حقه على الكمال لأن حق كل واحد منهما في عدم الحياة وبقتل الواحد حصل لهما إعدام الحياة معنى لما بينا. وإن حضر أحدهما والآخر غائب كان للحاضر أن يستوفي القصاص لأن كل واحد في إتلاف كل النفس واستيفاء البعض لمكان المزاحمة ولا مزاحمة هنا لأن حق الحاضر قد ظهر عند القاضي وحق الغائب لم يظهر وصار كأحد الشفيعين إذا حضر فقضى له بالجميع فكذا هذا
I killed my enemy killed. And in Al-Muhit: When one person kills two men, he is retaliated against for both, and he does not pay the blood money, because by his killing, each of them becomes fully entitled to his right, because the right of each of them is in non-existence of life, and by the killing of the one, the non-existence of life is achieved for both in meaning as we explained. And if one of them is present and the other is absent, the one present may execute the retaliation, because each one is [entitled] to destroy the entire life, and the partial execution is due to competition, and there is no competition here because the right of the present one has appeared before the judge, and the right of the absent one has not appeared, and it becomes like one of the two co-pre-emptors when he is present and judgment is given to him for the whole; thus this.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Kitab al-Jinaayat on qisas for killing two people. The commentator cites Al-Muhit to explain that if one person kills two, he is subject to qisas (retaliatory execution) for both, and no blood money is due. The reason is that each victim's right is in the killer's death, and one death satisfies both rights completely. If one victim's heir is present and the other is absent, the present heir may execute qisas immediately because his right is established before the judge while the absent one's right is not yet established, similar to the case of two co-pre-emptors (shuf'a) where the present one can take the entire property. This is a Hanafi position based on rational analogy and the principle of priority of established rights.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment in kind, e.g., taking life for life or limb for limb.
مزاحمة (muzahamah): Competition between two rights-holders over the same subject.
شفيع (shafi'): Co-pre-emptor who has the right to buy out the other co-owner's share if sold to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If one person kills two people, what is the ruling on qisas according to the Hanafi school?
A) Qisas is executed for both, and blood money is also due.
B) Qisas is executed for both, and no blood money is due.
C) Qisas is not possible because two cannot be killed for one death.
D) Only one qisas is executed, and blood money is paid for the other.

Key Takeaway
One intentional killer of two is subjected to a single qisas execution that fully satisfies both victims' rights, and the present heir may execute immediately by analogy with shuf'a.

Qisas and Diyah for Hand Cutting: Joint Rights
Rules for retaliation when two victims claim one hand cut

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for limb injuries when two victims share a right, and explain the difference from homicide.

Core Idea
In limb injuries, compensation for the second victim is wajib even if only one limb exists; unlike homicide, deficiency does not bar diyah.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explores a nuanced scenario: two victims both claim that the offender cut each of their hands, but only one hand is available for retaliation. How does the law balance their rights? The text compares this to homicide, where one execution satisfies all rightful claimants, and then examines the effects of forgiveness and payment. Understanding this rule clarifies the Hanafi emphasis on preserving qisas where possible while ensuring no right goes uncompensated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and ruling
Read the opening: 'If there were the cutting of the two hands for them, and he cut for one of them...' The question is: what happens to the second victim? The ruling is that he gets diyah (blood money) of his hand.
This is the default position before any forgiveness or payment.
2
Contrast with homicide
The text explicitly contrasts limb cases with homicide ('unlike the qisas for the life...'). In homicide, if one person kills and multiple heirs have the right to qisas, executing one satisfies all. In limb cases, the second victim's right to compensation remains because the deficiency (only one hand) does not extinguish his right.
The reasoning is aqli (rational): the loss of opportunity to execute does not negate the right to financial compensation.
3
Examine the effect of forgiveness before and after judgment
If one victim forgives before judgment, the other can still get qisas because the competition (muzahama) is removed. If forgiveness occurs after judgment and diyah has been taken, the remaining victim gets half the diyah (they shared it).
This shows that after judgment, the right to qisas transforms into a right to diyah, and forgiveness affects the distribution.
4
Address the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Muhammad holds that if one victim already took diyah (or killed and cut another), the silent one gets diyah. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the silent one may still cut the hand. The text sides with the Sahibayn: 'قالا' indicates their view is stronger.
The preferred (mufta bihi) position is that qisas remains possible as long as the right to execute has not been fully exhausted.
5
Apply the rule of kafalah (guarantee)
If diyah was taken through a guarantor (kafil), then forgiveness by one does not prevent the other from qisas because the guarantee is a suspension that preserves the original right.
This further demonstrates the Hanafi preference for keeping qisas alive when possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان قطع اليدين لهما فقطع لأحدهما والمسألة بحالها فللآخر دية يده بخلاف القصاص بالنفس إذا قضى لأحدهما وقتله لم يجب للآخر شيء لأن فوات حقه في الاستيفاء يكون سبباً لقصور في المحل فإنهما إذا اجتمعا واستوفيا صار كل واحد منهما مستوفياً حقه على الكمال فلا تجب معه الدية، وإما في الطرف فوات حقه بسبب قصور في المحل لا يضر عن إيفاء حق كل واحد منهما فيجب الضمان . ولو عفا أحدهما قبل القضاء بالقصاص أو الدية بطل حقه واقتص للآخر لأن المزاحمة قد انقطعت بالعفو فبقي حق الآخر في الكل، وإن عفا بعد القضاء بالقصاص وصالح ولي المقتول فالدية بينهما، فلو قتل وقطع اليد من آخر وأخذ الدية فللساكت دية اليد عند محمد. وقالا : للساكت أن يقطع اليد على أن لهما حق استيفاء القصاص في يد واحدة واستيفاء دية واحدة ولا قصاص مع وجود الموافقة والملائمة وانعدام المنازعة والمشاجرة ولكنه أقصى ما يجب لهما وهو أن يجتمعا على القطع وأخذ الدية بينهما فصار الحال بعد القضاء كالحال قبله . ولو أخذ الدية عن اليد ثم عفا أحدهما يكون للآخر نصف الدية لأنهما لما قبضا الدية فقد ملكاها، ومن ضرورة ثبوت الملك في المستوفي أن لا يبقى الحق في اليد فسقط حق كل واحد منهما في نصف اليد كيلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك واحد فلا يتمكن من استيفاء كل اليد بدون نصيب العافي فبطل حقه في القصاص فامتنع القطع لأن من موجبه الدية في نصيبه كما إذا كان خطأ، ولو أخذ الدية كفيلاً ثم عفا أحدهما فللآخر القصاص لأن الكفالة توقيف .
And if there were the cutting of the two hands for them, and he cut for one of them, and the case is as it was, then for the other is the blood money of his hand, unlike the qisas for the life when judgment is given for one of them and he kills him, nothing is owed to the other because the loss of his right to execute is due to a deficiency in the subject; for when they unite and both execute, each becomes fully entitled to his right, so blood money is not obligatory with it. But in the case of a limb, the loss of his right due to a deficiency in the subject does not harm the fulfillment of each one's right, so compensation is obligatory. And if one of them forgives before judgment of qisas or blood money, his right is nullified and the other executes qisas because the competition has been removed by the forgiveness, so the other's right remains in the whole. And if he forgives after judgment of qisas and the guardian of the slain makes a settlement, then the blood money is between them. So if he killed and cut the hand of another and took the blood money, then according to Muhammad, the silent one gets the blood money of the hand. And both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the silent one may cut the hand, because they have the right to execute qisas on one hand and take one blood money, and there is no qisas with agreement and harmony and absence of conflict and quarrel, but that is the maximum that is obligatory for them, which is that they unite on the cutting and take the blood money between them, so the situation after judgment becomes like before it. And if he took the blood money for the hand and then one of them forgives, then for the other is half the blood money, because when they both received the blood money, they owned it, and from the necessity of establishing ownership in what is taken is that the right in the hand does not remain, so each one's right in half the hand falls, in order that the substitute and the substituted do not combine in one ownership, so he is not able to execute the whole of the hand without the share of the one who forgave, so his right in qisas is nullified, so cutting is prevented, because from its requirements is blood money for his share, as if it were accidental. And if he took the blood money in guarantee and then one of them forgives, then for the other is qisas, because guarantee is a suspension.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage discusses retaliation for cutting limbs when two victims share a right to qisas for one hand. If only one hand is cut for one victim, the other gets the blood money (diyah) of his hand. This differs from homicide where one execution satisfies both fully. In limb cases, the loss of right due to deficiency in the subject (i.e., only one hand available) does not negate compensation. The passage then covers scenarios of forgiveness: if a victim forgives before judgment, the other can still get qisas; if after judgment, diyah is shared. There is an intra-madhhab difference: Muhammad holds that if one killed and cut the hand of another and took diyah, the silent one gets diyah for the hand, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold the silent one can still cut the hand. If diyah was taken through a guarantee (kafil), forgiveness by one does not prevent the other from qisas. This shows the Hanafi preference for preserving the right of qisas as much as possible.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Monetary compensation paid to the victim or heirs for injury or death, replacing qisas when applicable.
كفالة (kafalah): Guarantee or surety; here refers to taking the diyah through a guarantor.
قصاص (qisas): Retributive punishment equal to the injury inflicted, applied in cases of intentional killing or bodily harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if two victims of hand-cutting both forgive after taking diyah, what happens to the amount of diyah?
A) Each gets the full diyah for the hand.
B) The diyah is divided equally between them.
C) The diyah goes to the state treasury.
D) The offender is relieved of all payment.

Key Takeaway
In joint limb injury claims, the Hanafi school ensures that no right is lost: the second victim always receives either qisas or diyah, with a preference for preserving qisas.

Successive Cuts with Healing: Qisas and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Muhammad's rulings on repeated cuts with intervals of healing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for successive cuts to a finger joint when healing has intervened, distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the principle of equality to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Healing between successive cuts resets equality; qisas requires exact match at the time of the second cut.

This passage from Imam Muhammad's al-Ziyadat presents a series of rulings on repeated injuries with intervals of healing. The central question is whether qisas (retaliation) is possible when the victim's wound has healed before a second cut. The Hanafi school insists on precise equality (musāwāh) at the moment of retaliation, so the interval of healing fundamentally alters the calculus. We will explore the main scenario of two finger joints cut separately, the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two students, and how the principle extends to other body parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The passage begins with a man cutting the upper joint of another's finger. It heals. Then he cuts the second joint. Both are intentional. The victim seeks qisas.
2
State the ruling and the ikhtilaf
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the cutter's finger is cut from the lower joint (matching the second cut), and it is treated as if both joints were cut at once. Abu Hanifa holds that the victim may only cut the cutter's upper joint.
The author notes that 'some masters' attribute the first view to both, implying it is the relied-upon position.
3
Examine the underlying condition: musawah (equality)
At the moment of the second cut, the victim's finger lacks the upper joint (it healed). The cutter's finger is whole. To achieve equality, the Sahibayn treat both cuts as one continuous act, allowing the lower joint to be cut. Abu Hanifa sees the healing as resetting equality, so only the upper joint of the cutter corresponds.
The text says: 'يقطع أصبع القاطع من المفصل الأسفل ويجعل كأنه قطع المفصلين بدفعة واحدة'.
4
Apply the principle to analogous cases
The passage extends the reasoning to other parts: half a joint with healing → no qisas; fingers then palm with no healing → qisas for entire hand; with healing → qisas for fingers plus hukumat 'adl for palm. Similarly for the glans and remaining penis.
Note the distinction: when healing intervenes, the later injury is judged separately and compensation (diyah or hukumat) may substitute if qisas is impossible.
5
Determine when healing intervenes vs not
If healing did not intervene between cuts, the entire injury is considered one continuous act, and qisas applies to the whole (e.g., removing whole hand). If healing did intervene, each cut is evaluated independently: qisas if equality exists for that cut, otherwise compensation.
This guides the judge in deciding whether to apply qisas or monetary compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد رحمه الله في الزيادات : رجل قطع المفصل الأعلى من أصبع رجل وبرأ منه ثم عاد وقطع الثاني أيضاً ثم اختصما إلى القاضي فالقاضي يقضي على القاطع بالقصاص في المفصل الثاني. هذا الذي ذكرنا إذا قطع المفصل الأعلى وبرئ ثم عاد وقطع المفصل الثاني فإنه يقطع أصبع القاطع من المفصل الأسفل ويجعل كأنه قطع المفصلين بدفعة واحدة، فمن مشايخنا من قال ما ذكر ههنا قولهما، أما على قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله للمقطوع مفصلاه أن يقطع المفصل الأعلى ثم قال هذا قول الكل، ولو قطع المفصل الأعلى واقتص من القاطع ثم وقطع المفصل الثاني وبرئ يجب لوجود المساواة، فرق بين . هذا وبين ر رجلين مقطوعي الأصابع قطع أحدهما كف صاحبه لا يقطع كف القاطع. أقول : فيه نظر لأن المساواة ممكنة فينبغي يقطع لإمكانها فتدبره، وكذا إذا كان مقطوع الكف قطع أحدهما زند صاحبه لا يقطع زند القاطع. ولو قطع من أصبع رجل نصف مفصل وكسر وبرئ ثم قطع ما بقي من المفصل وبرئ فلا قصاص عليه في شيء من ذلك، أما في النصف الأول فلحلول الجناية في العظم، وأما في النصف الثاني فلعدم المساواة لأن أصبع القاطع حال ما قطع الثاني من المفصل صحيحة والأصبع المقطوعة من نصف المفصل ناقصة ولو لم يحل بينهما برئ يجب القصاص في المفصل وجعل كأنه قطع المفصل بدفعة واحدة وكذلك لو قطع الأصابع من رجل وعاد وقطع الكف إن لم يحل بينهما برء يجب القصاص في يد كأنه قطع الكل دفعة واحدة وإن حال بينهما برء يجب القصاص في الأصابع وحكومة عدل في الكف، وكذا إذا قطع حشفة إنسان ثم عاد وقطع باقي الذكر إن كان قبل تخلل البرء تجب دية واحدة، وإن كان تخلل بينهما برء يجب كمال الدية في الحشفة وحكومة عدل في الباقي.
Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said in al-Ziyadat: A man cut the upper joint of a man's finger, and it healed, then he returned and cut the second as well, then they disputed before the judge, so the judge rules qisas on the cutter for the second joint. This which we have mentioned: if he cut the upper joint and it healed, then returned and cut the second joint, then he cuts the cutter's finger from the lower joint and makes it as if he cut both joints in one stroke. Some of our masters said: what is mentioned here is the view of both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for the view of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, for the one whose two joints are cut, he may cut the upper joint, then he said: this is the view of all. And if he cut the upper joint and qisas was taken from the cutter, then he returned and cut the second joint and it healed, qisas is obligatory due to the existence of equality. There is a difference between this and the case of two men whose fingers are cut, one cut the palm of the other: the cutter's palm is not cut. I say: there is consideration in it because equality is possible, so it should be cut due to its possibility, so reflect. Likewise, if one whose palm was cut cut the forearm of his companion, the cutter's forearm is not cut. And if he cut half a joint of a man's finger and it broke and healed, then cut the remainder of the joint and it healed, there is no qisas on him in any of that. As for the first half, because the injury was in the bone, and as for the second half, because of lack of equality: the cutter's finger at the time of cutting the second part of the joint was healthy, while the cut finger was deficient from half the joint. And if healing did not intervene between them, qisas is obligatory for the joint and it is as if he cut the joint in one stroke. Likewise, if he cut the fingers of a man and then returned and cut the palm, if healing did not intervene between them, qisas is obligatory for the hand as if he cut the whole in one stroke; if healing intervened between them, qisas is obligatory for the fingers and a fair compensation for the palm. Likewise, if he cut a man's glans and then returned and cut the remainder of the penis, if it was before the intervention of healing, one full blood money is due; if healing intervened between them, the full blood money is due for the glans and a fair compensation for the remainder.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings from Muhammad al-Shaybani's 'al-Ziyadat' concerning successive cuts to the same body part with intervals of healing. The key principle is that if a wound heals completely between cuts, each cut is treated as a separate injury, and qisas is only possible if the later cut can be matched exactly (equal in degree). For example, if the upper finger joint is cut, heals, then the next joint is cut, the cutter's finger is cut from the lower joint (i.e., the same level as the second cut) and it is considered as if both joints were cut at once. However, there is a difference of opinion: some attribute this to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, while Abu Hanifa is said to allow the victim to cut only the upper joint. The passage also addresses a case where a person with missing fingers cuts another's palm: the cutter's palm is not cut because equality is not possible. Similarly, half a joint then the other half: if healing intervened, no qisas because the cutter's finger is now whole while the victim's is deficient. The overarching principle is that qisas requires exact equality in extent and condition at the time of the second cut. If healing did not intervene, the entire injury is considered one continuous act, and qisas is for the whole. The passage ends with analogous cases for penis and fingers. This reflects the Hanafi insistence on precise equality (musāwāh) in limb retaliation.

Key Terms
برء (bur'): Healing of a wound; the interval of recovery between two injuries.
حكومة عدل (hukūmat 'adl): Fair compensation assessed by a judge for injuries not covered by fixed diyah amounts.
مساواة (musāwāh): Equality; the requirement that qisas is only applicable when the retaliation can be exactly equal to the injury in degree and location.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone cuts the upper joint of a victim's finger, it heals, then cuts the middle joint, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling on qisas?
A) No qisas; diyah for both joints.
B) The cutter's upper joint is cut.
C) The cutter's finger is cut from the lower joint, treating as one stroke.
D) The victim can choose to cut either joint.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qisas, healing between successive cuts does not automatically bar retaliation; the judge must assess whether equality can be achieved, and the Sahibayn allow cutting the lower joint of the cutter to match the total loss.

Qisas for Partial Joint Cuts: Healing Condition
First ruling in the qisas chapter on partial joint cuts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a half-joint cut is made before healing and explain why only arsh applies.

Core Idea
No qisas for a partial cut made before the part heals — only arsh is due.

This short ruling from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a specific scenario in qisas: a man cuts half of the second joint of a finger before the initial wound has fully healed (bar'). After the cut heals, the law treats it as if the cut was made from the start on that joint. Since cutting half the second joint in the first place does not mandate qisas (only monetary compensation), the same applies here. Understanding this rule hinges on grasping the healing condition (bar') as a prerequisite for qisas on limbs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Timing
The act is cutting half of the second joint. The key timing: the cut occurs 'before healing' (qabl al-bar'). Note that the wound from a prior injury had not yet healed.
The text says 'fa qabla al-bar’i qata‘a nisfa min al-mafsali al-thani' — the cut happens before healing.
2
Determine the Consequence After Healing
After the cut heals (thumma bari'a), the ruling is that it is considered as if from the beginning he cut half of the second joint (ka'annahu min al-ibtida' qata‘a nisfa min al-mafsali al-thani).
The law retroactively treats the act as an initial cut of that part.
3
Recall the Ruling for the Initial Case
In the hypothetical initial case of cutting half the second joint (without a prior wound), the ruling is that qisas is not obligatory; only arsh (compensation) is required. The text states 'wa hunāka lā yajibu al-qaṣāṣu bal yajibu al-arsh'.
This is the background rule from the chapter on qisas.
4
Apply the Analogy (Qiyas)
By analogy (qiyas jaliy), the present case is equated to the initial case. Since the condition for qisas (healing of the prior part) was not met, the ruling matches — no qisas, only arsh.
The text concludes 'fa hādhā dhālik' — so this is that.
5
Extract the Legal Principle
The underlying principle: qisas on a limb requires that the part being cut is sound and healed from any prior injury. If a cut is made before healing, it cannot be retaliated in kind; only monetary compensation is due.
This reinforces the condition of bar' (healing) for qisas in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل فقبل البرء قطع النصف من المفصل الثاني ثم برئ القصاص وجعل كأنه من الابتداء قطع النصف من المفصل الثاني وهناك لا يجب القصاص بل يجب الأرش فهذا ذلك
A man: before healing, he cut half of the second joint, then he healed, (there is) qisas, and it is considered as if from the beginning he cut half of the second joint, and in that case qisas is not obligatory but arsh is obligatory, so this is that.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This ruling deals with a scenario where a person cuts half of the second joint of a finger before the first wound has healed. After the cut heals, the rule is that it is treated as if the entire cut was made initially from the second joint. Since cutting half of the second joint in the initial case does not require qisas (only arsh), the same applies here: no qisas, only arsh (compensation). The reasoning is that the healing condition for qisas is not met because the cut was made before proper healing of the prior injury.

Key Terms
البرء (al-bar'): Healing of a wound; specifically, complete recovery of the injured part.
الأرش (al-arsh): Monetary compensation for a bodily injury; assessed by value (hukuma 'adl) or fixed amounts.
القصاص (al-qaṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment; inflicting exactly the same injury as suffered by the victim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man cuts half of the second joint before healing, what is the consequence?
A) Qisas is obligatory
B) Only arsh is required
C) No liability
D) Diyah is due

Key Takeaway
The healing condition (bar') is essential for qisas on limbs; cutting half a joint before healing results only in arsh.

Ruling: Healing from First Cut Then Second Joint Cut
Second scenario: healing completed before subsequent cut

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of qisas and arsh when a first cut heals before a second cut on the same digit, and identify the condition that triggers qisas on the upper joint.

Core Idea
Healing shifts qisas to the upper joint for a subsequent cut.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi jinayat (injuries): after a first cut has healed, a second cut is made on the same finger. The ruling involves two components: qisas (retaliation) on the upper joint and half of the arsh (blood money) for the second cut. Understanding this case clarifies how conditions affect the locus of retaliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two events
Note the sequence: healing from the first cut, then a second cut on half of the second joint. The word 'barʾa' (healed) and 'qataʿa' (cut) are key.
Healing is a condition; the second cut is a separate act.
2
Recognize the condition for qisas
The phrase 'liwujud al-shart' explicitly states that healing is the condition (shart) that makes qisas obligatory.
The condition is complete healing of the first wound.
3
Determine the locus of qisas
Qisas is on 'al-mafsil al-aʿla' (the upper joint) – the joint above the one that was cut.
The exact joint is unavailable because it was already cut and healed.
4
Identify the additional compensation
Half of the arsh (blood money) is due for the second cut itself, because only half the joint was cut.
Arsh is proportional to the extent of injury.
5
Understand the rationale
The lower joint is missing, so retaliation shifts to the nearest corresponding part to ensure proportionality.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of al-qisas bi al-mithl (like-for-like).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو برأ من القطع الأول ثم قطع النصف من المفصل الثاني يجب القصاص في المفصل الأعلى لوجود الشرط ويجب نصف الأرش في الثاني
And if he healed from the first cut, then he cut half of the second joint, qisas is obligatory in the upper joint because the condition exists, and half of the arsh is obligatory for the second.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This is a contrasting scenario: the first wound healed completely before the second cut. Here, since healing (bar') is complete, the condition for qisas is met. For the cut of the second joint, qisas is applied on the upper joint (i.e., the joint above the cut). Additionally, half of the arsh is due for the second cut itself. The reasoning is that the upper joint is analogous to the degree of injury, and the second cut, being after healing, triggers qisas on the upper joint because the lower joint is already gone.

Key Terms
المفصل الأعلى (al-mafsil al-a'la): The upper joint (proximal joint) relative to the cut; e.g., if second joint is cut, qisas on first (knuckle).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the first cut heals before the second cut
B) When the second cut is made before healing
C) Always for any joint cut
D) Only if both cuts are intentional

Key Takeaway
Qisas on the upper joint plus half arsh applies when the first cut heals before a second cut on the same digit.

Multiple Cutters and Liability for Death: Hanafi vs. Zufar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when two cutters cause death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on liability when two persons cut different limbs leading to death, explain the basis for distinguishing between them, and compare Zufar's dissenting view.

Core Idea
The last cutter nearest to the vital area bears full qisas; the earlier cutter pays only diyah for his injury.

In Hanafi criminal law, when multiple injuries contribute to death, the question arises: who is liable for the death? This passage from al-Zuhayriyyah presents a dispute within the Hanafi school itself. The three eponyms (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) assign capital liability to the second cutter whose wound is closer to the vital organs, while the first cutter merely compensates for his specific cut. Zufar, a notable Hanafi jurist, argues that if no healing intervened, both should share the blood money. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the passage and note the two injuries: first cut on the palm, second cut on the elbow. The victim dies.
2
State the Hanafi Position
According to the three scholars, the second cutter (elbow) is liable for qisas al-nafs (capital punishment), while the first cutter (palm) pays diyat al-qat' (compensation for the cut only).
This holds if both cuts were intentional.
3
Understand the Basis
The second cut is closer to the vital area (elbow nearer to the trunk). The first cut alone would not normally cause death. Hence the second cut is considered the direct cause of death.
Causation determines liability.
4
Contrast Zufar's View
Zufar holds that if no healing occurred between the cuts, both contributed to death. Therefore, whether intentional or accidental, both should share the full diyah of the soul equally.
Zufar focuses on combined contribution rather than proximity.
5
Extract the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: under the Hanafi view, the second cutter faces qisas (death penalty) and the first only pays diyah; under Zufar's view, both pay diyah (no qisas) and share it.
This affects punishment and financial liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : ولو قطع آخر كفه ثم قطع آخر مرفقه فمات، فإن كان عمداً فقصاص النفس على الثاني ودية القاطع على الأول وهذا قول علمائنا الثلاثة وقال زفر : إن كان عمداً وإن كان خطأ ولم يتخلل البرء فدية النفس عليهما
And in al-Zuhayriyyah: If one person cut his palm, then another cut his elbow, and he died, then if it was intentional, qisas for the soul is upon the second, and the diyah of the cutter is upon the first; this is the statement of our three scholars. And Zufar said: Whether it was intentional or accidental, if healing did not intervene, then the diyah of the soul is upon both of them.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school (with Zufar as a dissenter) regarding liability when two different persons inflict separate injuries that together cause death. The three eponyms (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that if the injuries are intentional, the second cutter (who cut the elbow, a more severe injury near life) is liable for qisas of the soul (death penalty), while the first cutter (palm) only pays diyah for his cut. Zufar argues that regardless of intent, if no healing occurred between the cuts, both are equally liable for the full diyah of the soul. The Hanafi position distinguishes based on causation: the second cut is the direct cause of death because it is closer to the vital area.

Key Terms
قصاص النفس (qisas al-nafs): Retaliation for taking a life, i.e., capital punishment.
دية القاطع (diyat al-qati'): The diyah (financial compensation) for the specific cut inflicted, not for death.
برء (bar'): Healing of the wound; if healing occurs between the cuts, the first cutter's liability is limited to his cut.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the three Hanafi imams, who faces qisas for death if one cuts palm and another cuts elbow?
A) The first cutter
B) The second cutter
C) Both equally
D) Neither if they are slaves

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when multiple injuries cause death, the one whose cut is closest to the vital area is solely liable for qisas.

Cutting the Hand of an Apostate: Liability Varies with Subsequent Status
Rulings on cutting hand of an apostate depending on his subsequent status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah for cutting an apostate's hand when the victim dies after returning to Islam, and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Liability for injuring an apostate depends on the victim's legal status at death, not at the time of injury.

This passage from Sharh al-Tahawi explores nuanced scenarios: if someone cuts the hand of a Muslim who later apostatizes and dies, or if the apostate returns to Islam. The ruling hinges on whether the victim's life was protected (ma'sum) at death. We will walk through three cases and the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
List the three distinct factual patterns presented: (1) cut while apostate, convert to Islam, then die; (2) cut while Muslim, apostatize, die as apostate; (3) cut while Muslim, apostatize, return to Islam, die.
2
Determine legal status at key moments
For each scenario, note when the act of cutting occurred relative to the victim's apostasy and death. Focus on whether the victim's life was protected at the time of death.
The protected status (isma) is restored upon return to Islam.
3
Apply the liability principle
If the victim's life is protected at death, full diyah (diyah of soul) is due. If unprotected, only diyah of the severed limb is due. If life was never protected (cut during apostasy and no return), no liability.
The principle is that the cutting itself is not punishable by qisas due to the victim's status; the diyah is based on the final status.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For scenario (3), Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that upon return to Islam, the life is fully protected, so full diyah is due. Muhammad argues that the cause of death is the hand injury, so only diyah of the hand is due. Note the difference.
The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي شرح الطحاوي ومن قطع يد مرتد فأسلم فمات فلا شيء على القاطع. ولو قطع يده وهو مسلم فارتد فمات فعليه دية اليد لا غير، ولو رجع إلى الإسلام ثم مات فعلى قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف عليه دية النفس، وفي قول محمد عليه دية اليد. وكذلك لو لحق بدار الحرب ولم يقض القاضي بلحوقه ثم عاد مسلماً فمات تجب دية اليد لا غير.
And in Sharh al-Tahawi: Whoever cuts the hand of an apostate and he (the apostate) becomes Muslim and then dies, there is nothing upon the cutter. If he cuts his hand while he is Muslim, then he apostatized and died, then upon him is the diyah of the hand only, no more. And if he returned to Islam and then died, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, upon him is the diyah of the soul, and according to Muhammad, upon him is the diyah of the hand. Similarly, if he joined the dar al-harb and the judge did not rule on his joining, then he returned as a Muslim and died, the diyah of the hand is obligatory, no more.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage discusses liability for cutting the hand of an apostate in various scenarios. If the victim was an apostate at the time of cut and later converted to Islam and then died, there is no liability because the apostate's life is not protected (non-Muslim combatant status). If the victim was Muslim at the time of cut, then apostatized and died as apostate, only diyah of the hand is due, not of the soul, because his life became unprotected after apostasy. However, if he returned to Islam before death, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say full diyah of the soul is due because his life is again protected, while Muhammad says only diyah of the hand as the direct injury. A similar ruling applies to joining dar al-harb without a judge's decision: only hand diyah.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): One who has renounced Islam; his life and property may be unprotected depending on status.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory of war (non-Muslim lands) where Islamic law does not apply to the apostate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim cuts the hand of a Muslim, then the victim apostatizes and dies as an apostate, what is due?
A) Full diyah of soul
B) Diyah of hand only
C) Nothing
D) Qisas

Key Takeaway
The protected status (isma) at the time of death determines the extent of diyah liability for an injury inflicted earlier.

Diyah for Hands: Right is Not Superior, and the Forearm Dispute
Rulings on diyah for hands and dispute on forearm compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah amount for loss of hands (both or one) and explain the Hanafi position on compensating the forearm when cut above the wrist.

Core Idea
Compensation for lost limbs is based on the type of benefit, not on functional superiority.

When a hand is accidentally cut off, Islamic law imposes a fixed diyah. But what if the cut occurs above the wrist, involving the forearm? The Hanafi school and others differ on whether additional compensation is due. This lesson explains the basic ruling for hands and the scholarly debate over the forearm, based on the principle that compensation is for the benefit (manfa'a) of the limb, not its degree of strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rulings for hands
First, note that for both hands accidentally cut, the full diyah is owed. For one hand, half diyah. Also, the right hand is not valued more than the left, even if it is stronger.
The justification is that the law considers the loss of the entire type of benefit (jins al-manfa'a), not a comparative superiority.
2
Examine the dispute regarding the forearm
When the hand is cut from the middle of the forearm, what compensation is due? Abu Hanifa (and al-Shafi'i) say: diyah for the hand plus hukuma 'adl (equitable estimate) for the remaining forearm. Abu Yusuf, Zufar, Malik, and Sufyan al-Thawri say: only the diyah for the hand, no extra for the forearm.
The difference hinges on whether the forearm has an independent benefit or is merely subservient to the hand.
3
Understand the Hanafi proof
The text states that the forearm has a separate benefit (e.g., supporting, pushing). Loss of that benefit is compensable, so hukuma is needed. The opposing view sees the forearm as a part of the hand; its loss is included in the hand's diyah.
This is a qiyas: the forearm is like other body parts without fixed diyah → requires hukuma.
4
Apply the dispute to higher cuts
The same difference applies if the cut is at the elbow or shoulder: Abu Hanifa's view is diyah for the hand (palm) plus hukuma for the upper arm, while Abu Yusuf's followers would give only diyah of the hand.
The principle is consistent: any part above the wrist is compensable by hukuma according to Abu Hanifa.
5
Identify the relied-upon position
The author declares that the correct position is that of Abu Hanifa. For fatwa, the mufta bihi is: diyah of the hand plus hukuma for the forearm or upper arm.
This tarjih is from the later Hanafi tradition, as recorded in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجعنا إلى بيان حكم الخطأ فنقول وبالله التوفيق : اليدين إذا قطعتا خطأ الدية لفوات جنس المنفعة على الكمال وفي أحدهما نصف الدية ولا تفضل اليمن على الشمال وإن كانت اليمين أكثر بطشاً من الشمال لأن العبرة في الجنايات لجنس المنفعة لا للزيادة، وفي اليد إذا قطعت من نصف الساعد دية اليد وحكومة عدل فيما وراء الكف وهو قول الحنفي والشافعي؛ روى صاحب الأمالي عن أبي يوسف أنه لا يجب في الساعد شيء وهو قول زفر ومالك وسفيان والثوري، وكذلك على هذا الاختلاف إذا قطع اليد من المرفق أو المنكب فإنه يجب في الكف دية اليد وحكومة العدل فيما وراء الكف. وعن أبي يوسف ومن تابعه في المسألة الأولى أنه يجب دية البد لا غير والصحيح قول أبي حنيفة
We return to explaining the ruling of error (unintentional): If both hands are cut by mistake, the full diyah (is due) because the loss of the type of benefit in its entirety; and for one of them, half diyah; and the right is not superior to the left even if the right is stronger in grip than the left, because the consideration in injuries is for the type of benefit, not the increase. And in the hand if it is cut from half of the forearm, the diyah of the hand and hukuma of justice in what is beyond the palm; this is the statement of the Hanafi and the Shafi'i. The author of al-Amali narrated from Abu Yusuf that nothing is obligatory in the forearm; this is the statement of Zufar, Malik, Sufyan, and al-Thawri. Similarly, upon this difference, if the hand is cut from the elbow or the shoulder, then the diyah of the hand is obligatory in the palm and hukuma of justice in what is beyond the palm. And from Abu Yusuf and those who followed him in the first issue, that only the diyah of the body (hand) is obligatory, but the correct is the statement of Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage covers the diyah (financial compensation) for hands lost by mistake (khat'). For both hands, full diyah; for one hand, half. There is no distinction between right and left, even if right is more useful, because compensation is based on the loss of the type of benefit, not degree. A dispute arises when the cut is above the wrist: if from mid-forearm, the Hanafi (Abu Hanifa) and Shafi'i hold that diyah for the hand is due plus hukuma 'adl (just compensation) for the forearm portion, because the forearm has independent benefit. Abu Yusuf, Zufar, Malik, Sufyan al-Thawri say nothing is due for the forearm itself, only diyah of the hand (since the forearm is considered part of the hand?). The correct relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa: additional compensation for the forearm. The same applies for cuts at elbow or shoulder: diyah for the hand and hukuma for the upper arm.

Key Terms
حكومة عدل (hukuma 'adl): A just estimation of compensation by experts, for injuries not covered by fixed diyah amounts.
جنس المنفعة (jins al-manfa'a): The type or kind of benefit; e.g., the overall functionality of a hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa), if a hand is cut from mid-forearm by mistake, what is due?
A) Diyah of hand only
B) Diyah of hand plus hukuma for forearm
C) Half diyah of hand for the hand, nothing for forearm
D) Full diyah for the whole arm

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the diyah for hands is based on loss of benefit type, not hand dominance, and the forearm requires additional hukuma compensation.

Joint Liability of Two Cutters for Diyah
Commentary on joint liability for two cutters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that two persons who jointly cause death by intentional cutting must each pay half the diyah from their own wealth, and explain the role of 'aqila in excluding intentional acts.

Core Idea
Two cutters each pay half diyah from own wealth because intentional acts exclude tribal liability.

In Hanafi fiqh, when two individuals each intentionally cut a limb of the same victim and the victim dies, the question arises who bears the diyah. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that both are jointly liable, each paying half the diyah from their own purse, as the 'aqila only covers accidental death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Two people each cut a limb of the same person, leading to death. The cuts are intentional.
The scenario assumes each cut independently contributes to death.
2
Determine the liability
The full diyah is required. Since both contributed, they split it equally: each pays half (i.e., a quarter of the full diyah each).
3
Identify the source of payment
Payment is from each cutter's personal wealth because the act was intentional; the 'aqila (tribal guarantor) only covers accidental killings.
This is a key Hanafi principle: intentional acts are not borne by 'aqila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وضمنا ديتها) أي ضمن القاطعان دية المقطوع لأن التلف حصل بفعلهما فيجب عليهما نصف الدية على كل واحد منهما الربع فتجب في مالهما لأن العاقلة لا تتحمل العمد .
He (the author) said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And both guaranteed her diyah) meaning the two cutters guarantee the diyah of the cut victim because the destruction occurred by both of their actions, so half of the diyah is obligatory upon each of them, a quarter upon each, and it is obligatory in their wealth because the 'aqila does not bear intentional acts.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is a commentary on the matn phrase 'wa damana diyataha' (they both guarantee her diyah). The commentator explains that when two persons each cut a limb (e.g., hands) and the victim dies as a result, both are liable for the full diyah of the victim: each pays half, i.e., a quarter of the full diyah each. This payment is from their personal wealth because intentional acts (here deliberate cutting) are not covered by the 'aqila (tribal guarantor), which only applies to accidental killings. The ruling is based on the principle that each contributed to the death directly.

Key Terms
ضمن (damina): To guarantee, be liable for; here, jointly responsible for payment.
عاقلة ('aqila): The group (tribe/kin) responsible for paying diyah in accidental killings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the two cutters pay the diyah from their own wealth and not from their 'aqila?
A) Because the cut was intentional
B) Because they are both slaves
C) Because the victim was a dhimmi
D) Because healing occurred

Key Takeaway
When multiple offenders jointly cause death by intentional acts, they share diyah proportionally and must pay from personal wealth.

12 min
Joint Qisas When One Cuts Hands of Two Victims
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one person cuts the right hands of two victims and explain the proof based on equality of cause.

Core Idea
Equal cause gives equal right to qisas, regardless of sequence.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses a specific scenario: one person intentionally cuts the right hand of two different victims. The Hanafi school rules that both victims collectively have the right to have the cutter's right hand cut (one hand for both) and share half the diyah. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, which hinges on the principle that equality in the cause (sabab al-istihqaq) generates equality in entitlement, and temporal precedence does not affect the right. We will also compare this with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act: one person cuts the right hand of two victims. Ruling: both victims jointly have the right to have the cutter's right hand cut, and they receive half the diyah (of a hand) divided equally.
The ruling applies whether the cuts were simultaneous or successive.
2
Examine the Shafi'i counter-position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if the cuts are successive, only the first victim gets qisas; the second receives arsh (compensation). The Hanafi response rejects priority.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (school difference).
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof: Qiyas on creditors
The Hanafi proof is analogical: Two creditors in a partnership share equally in the debtor's property regardless of whose debt was first. Similarly, both victims share equally in the right to the cutter's hand because their cause (the cut) is identical.
The illa is 'equality in the cause of entitlement'.
4
Distinguish from the case of rahn (pledge)
In rahn, the first pledgee's right excludes the second because it is a legal enforcement (istifa'). But qisas is based on direct causation, not enforcement, so priority does not bar the second.
This explains why the analogy to rahn fails.
5
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical
If the cutter is a slave owned by both victims, they equally share the right to enslave him—consistent with the principle that the cause gives equal rights.
This illustration reinforces the Hanafi reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وأن قطع واحد يميني رجلين فلهما قطع يمينه ونصف الدية) يعني إذا حضرا معاً سواء كان القطع جملة واحدة أو على التعاقب وقال الشافعي: إن قطعهما على التعاقب يقطع للأول منهما ويغرم أرش اليد للثاني. ولنا أن المساواة في سبب الاستحقاق يوجب المساواة في الاستحقاق ولا عبرة في التقدم والتأخر كالغريمين في الشركة. وهذا لأن حق كل واحد منهما ثابت في كل اليد لتقرر السبب في حق كل واحد منهما وهو القطع. وكونه مشغولاً بحق الأول لا يمنع تقرر السبب في حق الثاني، ولهذا لو كان القاطع لهما عبداً استويا في استحقاق رقبته، ولو كان يمنع بالأول لما شاركه الثاني بخلاف الرهن لأنه استيفاء حكماً فلا يثبت للثاني بعد ما ثبت للأول كالاستيفاء حقيقة، فإذا لم يمنع الأول بثبوت حق الثاني فيها استويا فيها يقطع لهما إذا حضرا معاً لعدم الأولوية ويقضى لهما بنصف الدية يقسمانه نصفين لاستوائهما فيه بخلاف ما إذا كان القصاص في النفس حيث يكتفي فيه
He (the author) said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And that one cut the right hands of two men, so for both of them is to cut his right hand and half the diyah) meaning if they both attend together, whether the cut was done in one go or successively. And al-Shafi'i said: If he cut them successively, the first one cuts his hand and he (the cutter) pays the arsh of the hand to the second. And for us (Hanafi), equality in the cause of entitlement necessitates equality in entitlement, and there is no consideration of precedence and lateness, like two creditors in a partnership. This is because the right of each one of them is established in the entire hand due to the establishment of the cause in favor of each one of them, which is the cut. And the fact that it is occupied by the right of the first does not prevent the establishment of the cause for the second. For this reason, if the cutter was a slave of both of them, they are equal in entitlement to his slavery. And if it were prevented by the first, the second would not share, unlike a pledge (rahn) because it is a legal enforcement; it is not established for the second after it has been established for the first, like actual enforcement. So since the first does not prevent the establishment of the right of the second in it, they are equal in it; so they cut (his hand) if they are both present together due to lack of priority, and judgment is given to them of half the diyah, which they divide in half due to their equality in it, unlike the case where qisas is in the soul where one suffices in it.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This commentary addresses a scenario where one person intentionally cuts the right hand of two different victims (e.g., two people). According to the Hanafi school, both victims together have the right to have the cutter's right hand cut (i.e., one hand for both), and they also share half the diyah (i.e., half the diyah of a hand, since only one hand is cut but two victims lost hands). The reasoning is that the cause (the cut) establishes a right for each victim in the cutter's hand equally, regardless of whether the cuts were simultaneous or successive. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that if successive, only the first victim gets qisas; the second gets arsh. But Hanafis analogize to two creditors in a partnership: both share equally in the debtor's property. Similarly, here both share the right to qisas on the one hand, so they must act together. They cannot cut two hands (only one). The half diyah is divided equally between them. This is contrasted with qisas for murder, where only one life can be taken even for multiple victims.

Key Terms
سبب الاستحقاق (sabab al-istihqaq): The cause of entitlement; here, the act of cutting gives right to qisas.
الرهن (al-rahn): Pledge/pawn; used as a counterexample where right of first excludes second.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi ruling, if one person cuts the right hand of two victims, how is qisas carried out?
A) The cutter's right hand is cut for the first victim only
B) The cutter loses both hands
C) The cutter's right hand is cut for both victims together
D) No qisas, only diyah

Key Takeaway
Equal cause (cut) gives both victims an equal right to the cutter's hand, so they share the single qisas and half the diyah equally.

Priority of the Present Claimant in Qisas
Hanafi legal reasoning on priority in multiple qisas claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a present qisas claimant may execute his right immediately, and state the compensation due to the absent claimant.

Core Idea
Certainty prevails over uncertainty: the present claimant's established right permits immediate qisas; the absent claimant gets diyah.

In cases where multiple claimants have a right to qisas for a limb, one may arrive before the other. The Hanafi school permits the present claimant to execute his right without delay, analogizing to the law of preemption (shuf'ah). This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling and its reasoning, including an intra-madhhab disagreement on the effect of forgiveness after a judicial decree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two claimants
Two claimants have a right to qisas for the offender's hand. One claimant arrives first and cuts the offender's hand. The passage rules that this is valid, and the absent claimant is entitled to half the diyah from the first claimant.
The present claimant's right is certain; the absent claimant's right is uncertain (he might forgive).
2
Understand the qiyas to shuf'ah
The ruling is derived by analogy to the case of two co-shafees when one is present and the other absent. The present shafee may take the whole property; the absent one cannot undo the transaction but may claim compensation from the present shafee.
The 'illa is the certainty of the present right versus the uncertainty of the absent right.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
If qisas was decreed by a judge and then one claimant forgives before execution, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the other still has qisas (forgiveness before enforcement is like forgiveness before decree). Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the decree creates a partnership, so forgiveness converts the right to arsh (compensation).
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
4
Apply the rule to wrongful excess cutting
If the first claimant cuts beyond the limit (e.g., from the elbow instead of the wrist), qisas for the original hand drops, but the first claimant still owes half the diyah, and the original offender (now the ‘cutter’) has options.
The diyah is not lost by the wrongful cut, because it was already obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد فقطع يده له فللآخر عليه نصف الدية لأن للحاضر أن يستوفي حقه ولا يجب عليه التأخير حتى يحضر الآخر ثبوت حقه بيقين وحق الآخر متردد لاحتمال أن لا يطلب أو يعفو مجاناً أو صلحاً فصار كأحد الشفيعين إذا حضر والآخر غائب حيث يقضى له بالشفعة في الكل لما قلنا . ثم إذا حضر الآخر بعد ما قطعت للآخر وطلب يقضى له بالدية لأن يده وفاؤها حق مستحق عليه فيضمنها لسلامتها له. ولو قضى بالقصاص بينهما ثم عفا أحدهما قبل استيفاء الدية فللآخر القود عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وعند محمد له الأرش لأن القصاص بالقضاء أثبت الشركة بينهما فعاد حق كل واحد منهما إلى البعض، فإذا عفا أحدهما فقد منع الآخر من استيفاء الكل. ولهما أن الإمضاء من في العقوبات فالعفو قبله كالعفو قبل القضاء، ولو قطع أحدهما يد القاطع من المرفق سقط القصاص لذهاب اليد التي فيها القصاص بالقطع ظلماً ولا ينقلب مالكاً كما إذا قطعها أجنبي أو سقطت بآفة سماوية، ولهما نصف الدية على حالها لأنها واجبة قبل قطعها ولا تسقط بالقطع ثم القاطع الأول بالخيار إن شاء قطع ذراع القاطع، وإن شاء ضمنه دية اليد وحكومة عدل في قطع الذراع إلى المرفق لأن يد القاطع كانت مقطوعة . الكف من حين قطع القاطع الأول من المرفق فكانت كالشلاء، وعلى هذا لو كان المقطوع يده واحداً فقطع القاطع من المرفق سقط حقه في القصاص ووجب عليه القصاص وللمقطوع من المرفق الخيار إن شاء قطع من المرفق، وإن شاء أخذ الأرش لما ذكرنا وقدمنا له مزيد بيان.
If one (of two claimants) cuts his (the offender's) hand, then the other has half the diyah against him. Because the present one may execute his right, and he is not obligated to delay until the other comes, as his right is established with certainty while the other's right is uncertain due to the possibility that he may not demand or may forgive gratuitously or by compromise. So it is like the case of one of two co-shafees when one is present and the other absent, where the present one is awarded the whole right of shafah as we said. Then when the other comes after the hand has been cut for the first, and he demands, he is awarded the diyah, because the hand's compensation is a right due upon him, so he guarantees it for its maintenance. If qisas was decreed between them, then one of them forgave before the diyah was executed, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf the other has the right of qisas; according to Muhammad he has the diyat al-arsh, because the decree of qisas established partnership between them, so the right of each reverted to a part, so when one forgave, he prevented the other from executing the whole. But for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the enforcement is among corporal punishments, so forgiveness before it is like forgiveness before the decree. If one of them cut the cutter's hand from the elbow, qisas is dropped due to the loss of the hand in which qisas was to be executed by wrongful cutting, and he does not become its owner, just as if a stranger had cut it or it had fallen due to a natural calamity. And they both have half the diyah in its current state because it was obligatory before the cutting and is not invalidated by the cutting. Then the first cutter has the option: if he wishes he may cut the cutter's forearm, or if he wishes he may demand the diyah of the hand and an equitable indemnity for cutting the forearm to the elbow, because the cutter's hand was already cut at the wrist since the first cutter cut it from the elbow, so it was like a paralyzed hand. Upon this, if the victim's hand was one and the cutter cut it from the elbow, his right to qisas drops and qisas becomes obligatory for him, and the one cut from the elbow has the choice: if he wishes he may cut from the elbow, or if he wishes he may take the arsh for what we mentioned, and we have already given more explanation of it.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the chapter on qisas below life. The passage explains a scenario where two claimants seek qisas for a hand, but one claimant arrives first and cuts the offender's hand before the other arrives. The ruling: the present claimant has the right to execute his qisas immediately, and if he does, the absent claimant is entitled to half the diyah from the first claimant, not from the offender. The reasoning is analogical to the case of two co-shafees in preemption (shuf'ah): the present shafee may take the whole property, and if the absent one later appears, he cannot undo the transaction but can claim compensation from the present shafee. Similarly, here the absent claimant's right is uncertain (he might forgive), so the present one is not delayed. If the absent later demands, he gets half the diyah. The passage also discusses: if both had qisas decreed then one forgives before execution, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the other still has qisas (as the forgiveness is before enforcement), while Muhammad says he gets arsh (since the decree created partnership). Additionally, if a claimant wrongfully cuts off the offender's hand from the elbow before qisas, the offender's right to qisas for the original hand is lost, but the claimant owes half the diyah; the original offender (now the cutter) has options to retaliate or take compensation.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliatory punishment in kind for intentional injuries or murder, based on the principle of equality.
الدية (al-diyah): Monetary compensation paid to the victim or his heirs instead of qisas.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of preemption in property, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold share before a stranger.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where one of two qisas claimants cuts the offender's hand before the other arrives, what is the absent claimant entitled to?
A) Nothing, because his chance is lost.
B) Half the diyah from the first claimant.
C) The full diyah from the offender.
D) Qisas on the first claimant's hand.

Key Takeaway
In multiple qisas claims, the present claimant may act immediately, and the absent claimant is compensated by the first claimant, not the offender.

One Act, Two Consequences: Intent and Error
Brief ruling: one arrow shot intentionally hits and passes through a man, then hits another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of ta'addud al-athar to determine the legal consequences when a single intentional act unintentionally hits a second victim.

Core Idea
A single intentional act can produce multiple legal effects: qisas for the intended target, diyah for the unintended.

Consider an archer who aims his arrow at one man with intent to kill, but the arrow passes through that man and strikes another. Is the archer liable for both? The Hanafi school teaches us that the legal classification of each injury depends on the intention toward each victim. This lesson explores the rule that a single physical act gives rise to multiple consequences based on its multiple effects, a principle known as ta'addud al-athar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two victims
The act: shooting an arrow intentionally at a specific man. The victims: the first (intended) and the second (unintended but hit by the same arrow).
Focus on the shooter's intention for each victim separately.
2
Classify each injury by intent
The first injury is 'amad (intentional) because the shooter aimed and shot at him. The second injury is 'khata' (error) because the shooter did not intend to hit the second person, even though the act of shooting was intentional.
In Hanafi law, the classification depends on the specific victim.
3
Apply the Hanafi penalty for each classification
For 'amad: qisas (retaliation) is wajib upon the shooter if the first victim dies. For khata': diyah (blood money) is wajib, paid by the shooter's 'aqila (tribal surety).
The penalties are distinct and do not cancel each other.
4
Understand the analogy given in the text
The text likens the second hit to shooting at a harbi (enemy combatant) and accidentally hitting a Muslim. In that case, the act is intentional in aim but erroneous in effect, so diyah applies. Similarly, the second victim here is treated as a khata'.
The analogy reinforces the principle that effect matters.
5
Derive the general principle: ta'addud al-athar
The ruling shows that a single physical act can have multiple legal consequences because its effects are multiple. The same arrow has two different impacts, each with its own legal classification.
This principle is used elsewhere in fiqh, e.g., in transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن رمى رجلاً عمداً فنفذ السهم منه إلى آخر يقتص للأول وللثاني الدية لأن الأول عمد والثاني أحد الخطأ فكأنه رمى إلى حربي وأصاب مسلماً والفعل الواحد يتعدد بتعدد أثره والله تعالى أعلم.
If he shoots a man intentionally and the arrow passes through him to another, qisas is (executed) for the first, and for the second the diyah, because the first is intentional and the second is (a type of) error (qati'). It is as if he shot at a harbi (enemy combatant) and hit a Muslim. A single act multiplies by the multiplicity of its effects. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is a brief ruling statement in the chapter on qisas. The scenario: a person intentionally shoots an arrow at one man, and the arrow passes through him and strikes another man, killing or injuring both. The ruling: the shooter is subject to qisas for the first victim (the intended target) because the act was intentional (amad), and he must pay diyah for the second victim because the hit on the second is considered a type of error (khata') — similar to the analogy of shooting at an enemy warrior (harbi) and accidentally hitting a Muslim, where the act is intentional in aim but erroneous in effect. The principle: a single physical act can produce multiple legal effects depending on its multiple results (ta'addud al-athar). Thus, two different liabilities arise from one action.

Key Terms
عمد (amad): Intentional act with intent to cause harm; here, the first hit is amad.
خطأ (khata'): Error or mistake; the second hit is considered khata' because it was not intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Qisas for both, because the act was intentional.
B) Qisas for the first and diyah for the second.
C) Diyah for both, because the action was one.
D) No liability for the second, because it was accidental.

Key Takeaway
A single intentional act can produce multiple legal consequences; the second, unintended victim is treated as error (khata') requiring diyah.
