Five Cases of One Guardian Testifying Against Another on 'Afw
Detailed enumeration of five subcases of guardian testimony on 'afw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the five subcases when one guardian testifies against another regarding forgiveness in qisas, and apply the Hanafi ruling for diyah liability in each.

Core Idea
A guardian's testimony about another's forgiveness can convert qisas into diyah, with distribution depending on who confirms or denies.

This passage from al-Mabsut presents a detailed scenario: two guardians of a victim, one testifies that the other has waived retaliation. The ruling on diyah varies across five subcases based on whether the other guardian and the killer confirm or deny the testimony. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for applying Hanafi law in qisas disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the testimony
Start with the basic setup: exactly two guardians (walis). One testifies that the other has forgiven (afw) the killer, thus waiving qisas. The passage then outlines five scenarios based on how the other guardian and the killer react.
The guardians share the right to qisas; forgiveness by one affects the whole.
2
Map the five subcases
Create a 2x2 grid: the other guardian (G2) and the killer (K) each can confirm (saddaqa) or deny (kadhdhaba) the testimony, or remain silent. This yields five distinct possibilities (including both silent). For each, determine whether forgiveness is effective and how the diyah is distributed.
Silence is treated as neither confirmation nor denial in this context.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule for each subcase
For each subcase, state the diyah liability: (a) both confirm → witness gets half diyah; (b) both deny → witness gets nothing, other guardian gets half; (c) G2 denies, K confirms → killer pays full diyah divided between both guardians (each half); (d) K denies, G2 confirms → killer pays half diyah to G2 only; (e) both silent → forgiveness effective, diyah consequences follow the same pattern as (a)? The text implies forgiveness is effective, but diyah ruling is not explicit; it likely follows (a) because the testimony is uncontested.
The key principle: the witness's admission that the other forgave causes his own share to convert to diyah; similarly, the other's share converts if the testimony is not contradicted.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar
Focus on subcase (d): killer denies, other guardian confirms. Zufar argues nothing is due because forgiveness is established between the two guardians but not against the killer. The Hanafi response uses a qiyas (analogy) with debt redirection: the killer's denial amounts to admitting the diyah for the other guardian, and that guardian's confirmation redirects it to the witness (since the witness is the one who 'owns' the claim). Understand this logical step and why the Hanafi view prevails.
The analogy: someone admits a debt for Zayd, Zayd says it is for Amr → the debt becomes for Amr. Here, the killer 'admits' diyah for G2 by denying the testimony, and G2's confirmation passes it to the witness.

وقال في المبسوط : له وليان اثنان فشهد أحدهما على صاحبه أنه عفا فهو على قسمين : إما أن يشهد أحدهما على صاحبه بالعفو أو يشهد كل واحد منهما على صاحبه بالعفو . أما القسم الأول فهو على خمسة أوجه : إما أن يصدقه صاحبه والقاتل جميعاً أو كذباه أو كذبه صاحبه وصدقه القاتل أو على عكسه أو سكتا جميعاً فالعفو واقع في المفصول كلها لأن الشاهد أقر بعفو صاحبه فقد أقر بسقوط القصاص في نصيبه، وإذا سقط يسقط في نصيب الآخر كما لو عفا الشاهد عن نصيبه . وأما الدية إن تصادقا فاللشاهد نصف الدية لأن الثابت بالتصادق والموافقة كالثابت بالمعاينة وإن كذباه فلا شيء للشاهد ويجب للآخر نصف الدية لأنه لما شهد بالعفو فقد أقر ببطلان حقه في القصاص فصح وادعی انقلاب نصيب نفسه مالاً فلم يصدق ويحول نصيب الآخر مالاً لأن تعذر استيفاء القصاص في نصيبه من جهة غيره لأن سقوط القصاص مضاف إلى شهادة بالعفو فكان بمنزلة العفو منه، وإن كذبه صاحبه وصدقه القاتل ضمن الدية بينهما لأنه لما صدقه فقد أقر له بنصف الدية فلزمه وادعى بطلان حق المشهود عليه بالعفو فلم يصدق نصيب الساكت مالاً لأن في زعم الشاهد أن نصيبه تحول مالاً بعفو صاحبه والقاتل صدقه فيه فوجب له نصف الدية على القاتل وفي نصيب صاحبه لم يسقط من جهته لأنه لم يثبت عفوه في حقه لتكذيبه وإنما سقط بإقرارا الشاهد فينقلب نصيبه مالاً. وإن كذبه القاتل وصدقه صاحبه ضمن نصف الدية للمشهود عليه ولا يضمن للشاهد شيئاً. وقال زفر : لا شيء لهما لأن العفو ثبت في حقهما بتصادقهما ولم يثبت في حق القاتل لتكذيبه فسقط نصيب الشاهد ولم يجب لتكذيب نصف الدية فيبرأ القاتل. ولنا أن القاتل لما أكذب الشاهد في الشهادة بالعفو فقد كذبه فيما ادعى عليه من نصف الدية وأقر للمشهود عليه بنصف الدية في ماله لأنه زعم أن نصيب المشهود عليه إنما سقط لمعنى جاء من قبل الشاهد لا من جهته فإنه أنكر عفو المشهود عليه والمشهود عليه لما صدق الشاهد في شهادته فقد أقر بذلك المال للشاهد والمقر له بالمال إذا قال المقر ما أقررت به ليس لي وإنما هو لفلان كان المقر به لفلان كمن أقر بمائة لزيد فقال زيد هي لعمر وصارت المائة لعمرو فكذا هذا.
And he said in al-Mabsut: There are two guardians for him, and one of them testified against his companion that he had forgiven. So it is of two divisions: either one testifies against his companion regarding forgiveness, or each one of them testifies against his companion regarding forgiveness. As for the first division, it has five aspects: either his companion and the killer both confirm him, or both deny him, or his companion denies him and the killer confirms him, or the opposite, or both remain silent. So forgiveness is effective in all the [cases] because the witness admitted the forgiveness of his companion, so he admitted the fall of retaliation in his share, and when it falls, it falls in the share of the other, just as if the witness had forgiven his own share. As for the blood money: if they both confirm him, the witness has half the blood money, because what is established by mutual confirmation and agreement is like what is established by eyewitness. If they both deny him, then nothing is for the witness, and half the blood money is obligatory for the other, because when he testified to forgiveness, he admitted the nullity of his own right to retaliation, so that is valid. And he claimed the conversion of his own share into wealth, so he is not believed, and the share of the other converts into wealth, because the possibility of exacting retaliation in his share is prevented by something from another, since the fall of retaliation is attributed to testimony of forgiveness, so it is like forgiveness from him. If his companion denies him and the killer confirms him, he is liable for the blood money between them, because when he (the killer) confirmed him, he admitted half the blood money for him, so it is binding on him, and he claimed the nullity of the right of the witnessed-against due to forgiveness, so he is not believed. The share of the silent one is wealth, because in the opinion of the witness, his share converted into wealth due to his companion's forgiveness, and the killer confirmed him in that, so half the blood money is obligatory on the killer for him. And in the share of his companion, it did not fall from his side, because his forgiveness was not established against him due to his denial; rather it fell due to the witness's admission, so his share converts into wealth. If the killer denies him and his companion confirms him, he (the killer) is liable for half the blood money for the witnessed-against, and he is not liable for anything for the witness. And Zufar said: Nothing is for either of them, because forgiveness was established between them through mutual confirmation, but it was not established against the killer due to his denial, so the witness's share fell, and half the blood money is not obligatory due to the denial, so the killer is free. And we (Hanafis) argue: The killer, when he declared the witness false in his testimony of forgiveness, he denied him regarding what he claimed of half the blood money, and he admitted half the blood money for the witnessed-against from his property, because he claimed that the share of the witnessed-against fell due to a reason coming from the witness, not from his side, for he denied the forgiveness of the witnessed-against. And the witnessed-against, when he confirmed the witness in his testimony, he admitted that wealth for the witness, and the person owed the wealth, if he says to the one who admitted, 'What you admitted is not for me, it is for so-and-so,' then what is admitted is for so-and-so, just as if someone admitted a hundred (dirhams) for Zayd, and Zayd said, 'It is for Amr,' then the hundred becomes for Amr. So it is here.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage from al-Mabsut discusses a scenario with exactly two guardians (walis) of the victim, where one testifies that the other has waived qisas. The text divides into two main divisions: (1) one testifies against the other, and (2) each testifies against the other. Only the first division is covered in this passage, which includes five subcases. The commentator explains each subcase: (a) Both the witnessed-against guardian and the killer confirm the testimony: the witness gets half the diyah. (b) Both deny: the witness gets nothing, the other guardian gets half. (c) The witnessed-against denies but the killer confirms: both guardians share the diyah (each gets half? The text says 'ضمن الدية بينهما' meaning the killer pays full diyah between them, i.e., each gets half). (d) The killer denies but the witnessed-against confirms: the killer pays half diyah to the witnessed-against only, nothing to the witness. (e) Both remain silent: not explicitly ruled here but it seems forgiveness is effective. The passage then addresses the opinion of Zufar, who says nothing is due to either guardian because the killer's denial prevents the conversion. The Hanafi rebuttal uses the analogy of debt redirection: the killer's denial of the witness's claim amounts to an admission for the witnessed-against, and the witnessed-against's confirmation redirects that payment to the witness.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): Guardian of the victim who has the right to claim qisas or diyah
شهادة (shahada): Testimony in court
سكوت (sukut): Silence of the parties, which does not imply admission or denial

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fifth subcase of al-Mabsut's first division (where the killer denies and the witnessed-against guardian confirms), what is the ruling?
A) The killer pays half diyah to the witness
B) The killer pays half diyah to the witnessed-against guardian
C) The killer pays full diyah to both guardians
D) The killer pays nothing

Key Takeaway
In cases of one guardian testifying against another on 'afw, the diyah liability is determined by the confirmation/denial pattern, with the Hanafi view using debt-redirection analogy to ensure justice.

Confession vs Testimony in Homicide: Acceptance of Multiple Claims
Expansion on the ruling of multiple confessions vs testimonies in homicide

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the acceptance of multiple confessions and multiple testimonies in homicide, and explain the underlying legal rationale based on the differing conditions for each.

Core Idea
A confession stands alone; a testimony depends on the integrity of witnesses—contradiction breaks it.

Why is it that two men can each confess to the same murder and the heir may execute both, yet if two sets of witnesses testify against two different men, the testimony is void? This lesson unpacks the key distinction: confession requires no external integrity check, while testimony depends on the witnesses being upright and consistent. The author of Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies this rule through a series of scenarios, revealing the subtle interplay between the heir's statement and the validity of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Ruling
Locate the core ruling: when two individuals each confess to killing Zayd alone, and the heir says 'both killed him,' the heir may kill both. In contrast, if two witnesses testify against one man and another two against another, the testimony is invalid.
The ruling hinges on whether the proof is a confession (iqrar) or testimony (shahada).
2
Understand the Heir's Statement
The heir's statement 'both killed him' is an implicit confirmation of each confession, but it also contradicts the claim that either acted alone. This contradiction is tolerated in confession but not in testimony.
Explicit confirmation ('you both spoke truthfully') has a different effect—it negates qisas.
3
Grasp the Legal Difference
Confession is valid even if the confessor is sinful (fasiq); testimony requires the witnesses to be 'adil (upright). The heir's statement implying shared killing impugns the witnesses' integrity, rendering their testimony void.
This is the key: the condition of 'adalah (integrity) applies to witnesses but not to the confessor.
4
Apply Variations
Analyze related cases: (a) if the heir explicitly confirms both confessors, qisas is lost; (b) if one confesses and another is testified against, only the confessor can be executed; (c) the same rules apply to unintentional killing.
Implicit confirmation (e.g., 'both killed you' in plural) is permissible; explicit confirmation is not.
5
Synthesize the Principle
The Hanafi principle is clear: the acceptance of multiple confessions is based on the strength of iqrar as a proof against oneself, while the rejection of multiple testimonies protects the integrity of the judicial process.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi law and has practical implications for court proceedings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن أقر كل واحد منهم أنه قتله وقال الولي قتلاه جميعاً له قتلهما ولو كان مكان الإقرار شهادة (لغت) يعني لو أقر رجلان كل واحد منهما أنه قتل زيد منفرداً فقال الولي قتلاه جميعاً له ،قتلهما، وإن شهد اثنان على رجل أنه قتله وشهد آخران على آخر أنه قتله بطلت الشهادة، والفرق بينهما أن كل واحد من الإقرار والشهادة يثبت أن كل القتل وجد من المقر والمشهود عليه ومقتضاه أن يجب القصاص عليه وحده لأن معنى قوله أنا قتلته انفردت بقتله وكذا قول الشهود قتله فلان يوجب انفراده بالقتل وقول الولي قتلهما تكذيب له حيث ادعى اشتراكهما في القتل فكأنه قال لم ينفرد أحد كما يقتله بل شاركه الآخر، وهذا القدر من التكذيب يمنع صحة قبول الشهادة لادعائه فسقهم به دون الإقرار لأن فسق المقر لا يمنع صحة الإقرار ولو قال في الإقرار صدقتما ليس له أن يقتل واحداً منهما لأن تصديق كل واحد منهما تكذيب للآخر لأن كل واحد منهما يدعي الانفراد بالقتل بتصديقه فوجب فصار كأنه قال لكل واحد منهما قتلت وحدك ولم يشاركك فيه أحد فيكون مقراً بأن الآخر لم يقتله بخلاف الأول وهو ما إذا قال قتلتماه تصديق لهما قلنا هو تصديق ضمني والضمني يتسامح فيه ما لا يتسامح في القصدي وهو قوله صدقتما ولو أقر رجل أنه قتله وقامت البينة على الآخر أنه قتله وقال الولي قتله كلاكما كان له أن يقتل المقر دون المشهود عليه لأن فيه تكذيباً لبعض موجبه على ما مر . وعلى هذا لو قال لأحد المقرين صدقت أنت قتلت وحدك كان له أن يقتله لأنهما تصادقا على وجوب القتل عليه وحده، وكذا إذا قال الأحد المشهود عليهما أنت قتلته كان له أن يقتله لعدم تكذيب المشهود له وإنما كذب الآخرين، وكذلك الحكم في الخطأ في جميع ما ذكرنا
He (the author) said: (If each one of them confesses that he killed him, and the heir says 'Both of them killed him,' then he may kill both; and if there were testimony in place of confession, it would be invalid.) Meaning: If two men, each one of them confesses that he killed Zayd alone, and the heir says 'Both of them killed him,' then he may kill both. But if two witnesses testify against one man that he killed him, and another two testify against another that he killed him, the testimony is invalid. The difference between them is that each of confession and testimony establishes that the entire killing originated from the confessor and the one testified against, and its consequence is that qisas must be applied to him alone, because the meaning of his statement 'I killed him' is that he alone killed him, and likewise the statement of the witnesses 'So-and-so killed him' necessitates his alone killing him. The heir's statement 'Both of them killed him' is a rejection (takdhib) of him, because he claims their sharing in the killing, so it is as if he said: 'Neither one killed him alone; rather, the other shared.' This amount of rejection prevents the validity of accepting the testimony due to his claim of their sinfulness, but not the confession, because the sinfulness of the confessor does not prevent the validity of the confession. And if he said in the confession 'You both have spoken truthfully,' he may not kill either of them, because confirming each one is a rejection of the other; since each of them claims exclusive killing, by confirming him it becomes as if he said to each: 'You killed alone and no one shared with you,' so he becomes confessing that the other did not kill him, contrary to the first case. That is when he says 'Both of you killed him'—we say it is an implicit confirmation, and tolerance is given to the implicit that is not given to the explicit, which is his statement 'You both spoke truth.' And if one man confessed that he killed him and the testimony of witnesses was established against another that he killed him, and the heir said 'Both of you killed him,' he may kill the confessor but not the one testified against, because there is rejection of part of his basis as previously. According to this, if he said to one of the confessors 'I believe you, you killed alone,' he may kill him, because they mutually affirm that killing is obligatory upon him alone. Likewise, if he said to one of those testified against 'You killed him,' he may kill him, because there is no rejection of the one testified for; he only rejected the others. And the same ruling applies in unintentional killing in all that we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding multiple confessions versus multiple testimonies in homicide. It opens by stating the rule: if two individuals each confess independently to the same murder, and the heir says both killed him, the heir may execute qisas on both. But if two witnesses testify against one person and another two testify against another, the testimony is void. The reason given is that each confession or testimony claims sole responsibility. The heir's statement that both killed contradicts this claim of sole responsibility, which is a form of impugning the witnesses' integrity (tazkiyah), thus invalidating their testimony. However, with confession, the confessor's credibility is not a condition, so the confession remains valid despite the contradiction. The commentary then examines variations: if the heir confirms both confessors, he cannot kill either because confirming each denies the other. If he says 'both killed you' (plural), that is implicit confirmation and acceptable. Cases where one confesses and the other is testified against, or where the heir specifies one confessor, are also analyzed. The same principles apply to unintentional homicide. The lesson carefully distinguishes between the legal effects of confessions and testimonies, based on the underlying principle that confession does not require external verification of integrity, while testimony does.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A voluntary admission or confession made by a person against himself, here specifically admitting to homicide.
شهادة (shahada): Testimony given by witnesses in court, which must meet strict conditions of integrity (tazkiyah) and consistency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where two men each confess to killing Zayd alone, and the heir says 'Both of you killed him,' what is the ruling according to this passage?
A) The confessions are invalid, and the heir cannot kill either.
B) The heir may kill both confessors.
C) The heir may kill only the first confessor.
D) The heir must take diyah from both.

Key Takeaway
Confession is accepted even with contradiction because it is a self-incriminating proof that does not require witness integrity, while testimony is voided by contradiction because it impugns the witnesses' uprightness.

Principle of Diyah When Qisas Is Impossible
Presents the rule for diyah when qisas cannot be executed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle for when diyah becomes obligatory when qisas cannot be executed, and apply it to scenarios of conflicting claims between the heir and the killer.

Core Idea
Diyah is due by istihsan when the impossibility of qisas is caused by the killer, but not when caused by the heir.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a fundamental principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding homicide: when retaliatory execution (qisas) cannot be carried out, blood money (diyah) may become obligatory depending on whose fault the impossibility arises. The passage then provides numerous illustrative cases (furu') that flesh out the principle, contrasting the outcomes under qiyas and istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule (asl)
Read the opening statement: 'al-asl anna ta'addhur istifa' al-qisas...' The principle distinguishes between impossibility due to the heir's side vs. the killer's side.
The rule is stated categorically at the start.
2
Distinguish causes from heir vs. killer
Note that if the cause is from the heir (e.g., he claims unintentional), diyah is not wajib. If from the killer (e.g., he confesses to intentional), diyah is wajib by istihsan.
This is the core distinction.
3
Understand the role of istihsan overriding qiyas
The text explicitly contrasts qiyas and istihsan. In many cases, qiyas would impose no liability, but istihsan imposes diyah for equitable reasons.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi method.
4
Analyze a branch case
Pick one branch, e.g., the case where heir claims unintentional and killer confesses intentional. Apply the principle: the impossibility is from the killer, so diyah is due by istihsan.
This illustrates the rule in action.
5
Recognize the pattern across scenarios
Explore other branches: multiple defendants, conflicting confessions, lack of knowledge about which injury caused death. Observe how the principle consistently guides the ruling.
The pattern reinforces the asl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الأصل ادعى الولي العمد أو الخطأ وصدق المدعى عليه أو كذب ويدخل فيه اختلاف الشاهدين؛ الأصل أن تعذر استيفاء القصاص بعد ظهور القتل إن كان لمعنى من جهة الولي لا تجب الدية، وإن كان لمعنى من جهة القاتل تجب الدية استحساناً فإنه يخرج على الأصل الذي قلنا فرع على ما إذا ادعى الولي الخطأ وأقر القاتل بالعمد فقال لو صدق الولي بعد ذلك القاتل وقال إنك قتلته عمداً فله الدية على القاتل بالعمد . . وعن أبي يوسف في نوادر ابن سماعة : إذا ادعى الولي الخطأ وأقر القاتل بالعمد فعلى القاتل الدية. وقال محمد رحمهما الله في الزيادات: ادعى رجل على رجلين أنهم قتلا وليه عمداً بحديدة فله عليهما القصاص فقال أحدهما صدقت وقال الآخر ضربته أنا خطأ بالعصا فإنه يقضى لولي القتل عليهما بالدية في مالهما في ثلاثة سنين وهذا الذي ذكرناه استحسان، والقياس أن لا يقضى عليهما بشيء. ولو ادعى الولي العمد عليهما وصدقه أحدهما في ذلك وأنكر الآخر القتل فلا شيء على المقر. وفي الخانية : ولو ادعى الخطأ عليهما وأقر أحدهما بالعمد وجحد الآخر فلم يقض بشيء، ولو ادعى العمد عليهما فأقر أحدهما وجحد الآخر القتل قتل المقر. ولو أقر أحدهما بالعمد والآخر بالخطأ وأنكر شركة الخاطئ قتل العامد، ولو قال رجل لرجل قتلت أنا وفلان وليك عمداً وقال فلان قتلناه خطأ وقال الولي للمقر بالعمد أنت قتلته وحدك عمداً فإن للولي أن يقتل المقر، وإن ادعى الولي الخطأ في هذه الصورة لا يجب شيء. رجل قطع يده ورجله ومات منهما فقال رجل قطعت يده عمداً وفلان قطع رجله ومات من ذلك وقال الولي لا بل أنت قطعت ذلك كله عمداً فإن للولي أن يقتله، وإن قال لا أدري من قطع رجله لا يكون له أن يقطع المقر وإن أزال الولي الجهالة بعد ذلك. وقال زفر : إذا بين صح بيانه حتى كان له أن يقتل المقر قال مشايخنا وهذا إذا بين الولي قبل أن يقضي القاضي ببطلان حقه في القصاص قبل المقر حيث قال لا أدري من قطع رجله، فأما إذا قضى بذلك ثم بين لا يصح بيانه ولا يكون له أن يقتل المقر. وفي نوادر بشر عن أبي يوسف: رجل قال لرجل أنا قتلت وليك عمداً فصدقه وقتله ثم جاء آخر وقال أنا الذي قتلته وحدي وصدقه فعليه دية الذي قتله وله على الآخر الدية. قال محمد رحمه الله في الزيادات : ادعى رجل على رجلين أنهما قتلا وليه عمداً بالسيف وقضى له عليهما بالقصاص فأقر أحدهما بالقتل وأقام آخر شاهدين على الآخر أنه قتله وحده عمداً كان للمدعي أن يقتل المقر مكان العمد وليس له أن يقتل المشهود عليه وبطلت شهادة شاهدين ولو كان مكان قتل العمد الخطأ وباقي المسألة بحالها لا شيء على المشهود عليه من الدية وعلى المقر نصف الدية وإن أقر بالكل. وفيها أيضاً : رجل قتل مقطوع اليدين وادعى وليه أن فلاناً قطع يده اليمنى عمداً وفلان قطع يده اليسرى عمداً ومات منهما فقال المدعى عليه أنا قطعت يده اليسرى عمداً ولا أدري من قطع يده اليمنى إلا أني أعلم أن اليمنى قطعت عمداً ومات من القطع وقال المدعى عليه قطعت اليد اليسرى ومات منها خاصة لا شيء على المقر، ولو قال الولي قطع فلان يده اليسرى عمداً ولا أدري من قطع اليمنى إلا أني أعلم أن اليمنى قطعت عمداً فمات منهما فلا قود عليه وعليه نصف الدية استحساناً والقياس أن لا يلزمه شيء من الدية. وفيها أيضاً: رجل ادعى على رجل أنه شج وليه موضحة عمداً ومات منها وجحد المدعى عليه ذلك فجاء المدعي بشاهدين فشهدا بالموضحة وبالموت منها كما ادعاه المدعي وشهد الآخر بالموضحة والبرء قبلت شهادتهما على الموضحة وقضى بالقصاص في الموضحة، فمن مشايخنا من قال ما ذكره من الجواب قول أبي يوسف ومحمد، أما على قول أبي حنيفة الله ينبغي أن لا تقبل هذه الشهادة ولا يقضى بشيء، ومنهم من قال لا بل هذا قول الكل. ولو ادعى الموضحة والبرء منها وشهد أحد الشاهدين بالموضحة والبرء والآخر بالسراية
And in the original: When the heir claims intentional or unintentional killing, and the defendant confirms or denies—and this includes the disagreement of witnesses—the principle is: if the impossibility of executing qisas after the killing is established is due to a cause from the side of the heir, diyah is not obligatory; but if it is due to a cause from the side of the killer, diyah is obligatory by istihsan. This deviates from the principle we stated. A branch on this: If the heir claimed unintentional killing, and the killer confessed to intentional, then if the heir later confirms the killer and says 'You killed him intentionally,' he has diyah from the intentional killer. And from Abu Yusuf in Nawadir Ibn Sam'a: If the heir claims unintentional and the killer confesses to intentional, diyah is due from the killer. Muhammad said in al-Ziyadat: A man sued two men that they killed his patron intentionally with an iron tool, and he has qisas against them; one said 'You are truthful,' and the other said 'I struck him unintentionally with a stick'; then the heir is awarded diyah from both in their wealth over three years—this is istihsan; by qiyas, nothing would be awarded. And if the heir claimed intentional against both, and one confirmed him while the other denied the killing, then nothing from the confessor. In al-Khaniya: If he claimed unintentional against both, and one confessed to intentional and the other denied, then nothing is awarded. If he claimed intentional against both, and one confessed and the other denied the killing, the confessor is killed. If one confessed to intentional and the other to unintentional, and the unintentional one denied partnership, the intentional one is killed. And if a man said to a man 'I and so-and-so killed your patron intentionally,' and so-and-so said 'We killed him unintentionally,' and the heir said to the intentional confessor 'You alone killed him intentionally,' then the heir may kill that confessor. If the heir claimed unintentional in this scenario, nothing is due. A man cut off his hand and his leg and he died from them; a man said 'I cut off his hand intentionally, and so-and-so cut off his leg and he died from that,' and the heir said 'No, you cut off all that intentionally,' then the heir may kill him. If he said 'I don't know who cut off his leg,' he may not cut off the confessor, even if the heir later removes the ignorance. Zufar said: If he explains, his explanation is valid so that he may kill the confessor. Our scholars said: This is if the heir explains before the judge rules that his right to qisas is nullified due to the confessor's statement 'I don't know who cut off his leg'; but if the judge rules that, then his explanation is invalid and he may not kill the confessor. In Nawadir Bishr from Abu Yusuf: A man said to a man 'I killed your patron intentionally,' so he confirmed him and killed him; then another came and said 'I alone killed him,' and he confirmed him; then the first is liable for diyah of the one he killed, and the other owes him diyah. Muhammad said in al-Ziyadat: A man sued two men that they killed his patron intentionally with a sword, and qisas was awarded against them; then one confessed to the killing, and another brought two witnesses that the other killed him alone intentionally; then the plaintiff may kill the confessor as intentional, but he may not kill the one testified against, and the testimony of the two witnesses is invalidated. If it were unintentional instead of intentional, and the rest of the case remains the same, then nothing is due from the one testified against in terms of diyah, and the confessor owes half diyah, even if he confessed to the whole. And also in it: A man with severed hands was killed, and his heir claimed that so-and-so cut off his right hand intentionally and so-and-so cut off his left hand intentionally, and he died from them; the defendant said 'I cut off his left hand intentionally, and I don't know who cut off his right hand, except that I know the right hand was cut intentionally and he died from the cut'; and the defendant said 'I cut off the left hand and he died from it alone,' then nothing is due from the confessor. If the heir said 'So-and-so cut off his left hand intentionally, and I don't know who cut off the right hand, except that I know the right hand was cut intentionally and he died from both,' then no qisas is due from him, and half diyah is due by istihsan; by qiyas, nothing is due from him. And also in it: A man sued another that he inflicted a scalp wound (mudiha) on his patron intentionally and he died from it, and the defendant denied; then the plaintiff brought two witnesses who testified to the mudiha and to the death from it as claimed, and the other testified to the mudiha and recovery; their testimony was accepted regarding the mudiha, and qisas was awarded for the mudiha. Some of our scholars said that the mentioned answer is the opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; as for Abu Hanifa, this testimony should not be accepted and nothing should be awarded. Others said that this is the opinion of all. And if he claimed mudiha and recovery, and one of the two witnesses testified to the mudiha and recovery, and the other to the fatal outcome...
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a general fiqh principle and numerous applications in homicide cases. It begins by stating the original principle (asl): if the inability to execute qisas (retaliatory execution) stems from the heir's action or claim, diyah (blood money) is not obligatory; if it stems from the killer’s action, diyah is obligatory by istihsan (juristic preference), deviating from qiyas. Then several furu' (branches) illustrate this. The first example: if the heir claims unintentional killing but the killer confesses to intentional, and the heir later confirms the intentional nature, diyah is due from the killer according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad's example in al-Ziyadat involves two defendants with conflicting confessions; by istihsan, both pay diyah over three years, while qiyas would impose nothing. Subsequent cases cover various combinations: claims of intentional vs unintentional, confessions vs denials, single or multiple defendants, and even separate injuries causing death. The principles of istihsan and qiyas are repeatedly contrasted. The passage also addresses issues of uncertainty about which injury caused death, including a hand-cut scenario and a scalp wound case with conflicting testimonies. The scalp wound case includes a ikhtilaf (disagreement) among the Hanafi imams: some attribute the ruling to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad only, while others say all three imams agree. The lesson demonstrates how the general rule is applied across many fact patterns, emphasizing the role of istihsan in providing equitable outcomes.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows a ruling to deviate from strict analogy (qiyas) for equity or practical convenience.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning, where a ruling from an original case is extended to a new case due to a shared effective cause ('illa).
دية (diya): Blood money or financial compensation paid to the heirs of a homicide victim, typically in lieu of qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle stated in this passage, if the impossibility of executing qisas is due to a cause from the heir's side, what is the ruling regarding diyah?
A) Diyah is obligatory.
B) Diyah is not obligatory.
C) The killer must be executed anyway.
D) The heir must pay diyah to the killer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school employs istihsan to ensure that when qisas is impossible due to the killer's action, diyah becomes obligatory, preventing the killer from escaping liability.

Conditions for the Slave in Kaffarah: Feeding, Fetus, and Suckling Infant
Rulings on qualifications of slave in kaffarah with proofs and rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rulings regarding the slave in kaffarah (feeding and fetus invalid, suckling infant valid under condition) and explain the Hanafi proof for each, including the distinction between apparent condition in expiation vs. liability.

Core Idea
A suckling infant is valid for kaffarah if one parent is Muslim, because apparent condition suffices for discharging an obligation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the precise conditions for a valid slave in kaffarah. The author rules out feeding and the fetus as valid, then permits the suckling infant under a specific condition, and addresses a subtle objection about reliance on apparent condition. Understanding these distinctions ensures the correct fulfillment of kaffarah obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases and their rulings
Read the two main parts: the author rules 'feeding and the fetus are not permissible' and 'the suckling infant is permissible if one of his parents is Muslim'. Note that feeding is not a slave, and the fetus is uncertain.
2
Trace the proof for the invalid cases
For feeding: the Qur'an mentions 'freeing a slave', not feeding; the fixed quantities are known only by revelation. For the fetus: its life and safety are unknown, and it is only a limb in one sense, so it does not fall under the absolute text.
The proof is based on the text (Quran) and lack of certainty.
3
Analyze the valid case: suckling infant
The infant is valid if one parent is Muslim because the infant follows the parent in Islam. The apparent condition (ghalabat al-zann) is that the infant's limbs are sound, based on habitual practice.
This relies on the principle that in discharging an obligation, apparent condition suffices.
4
Examine the objection and response
An objector asks: why accept apparent condition here but not in liability for destroying limbs? The author responds: in expiation, the need is to discharge an obligation, and apparent condition is a valid proof; in liability, the need is to avoid harm, which requires certainty.
The distinction lies in the purpose: fulfilling a duty vs. fixing harm.
5
Recognize the underlying qiyas
The infant is analogized to a believing, sound-limbed slave via the illa of being Muslim and apparently sound. This is a qiyas khafi using tahqiq al-manat.
The asl is the Quranic ruling to free a believing slave; the far' is the infant with one Muslim parent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولا يجوز الإطعام والجنين) لأن الإطعام لم يرد به النص والمقادير لم تعرف إلا سماعاً، ولأن المذكور كل الواجب أما في الجواب أو لكونه كل المذكور والجنين لم تعرف حياته ولا سلامته فلا يجوز ولأنه عضو من وجه فلا يدخل تحت مطلق النص. قال رحمه الله: (ويجوز الرضيع لو أحد أبويه مسلما) لأنه مسلم تبع له والظاهر سلامة أطرافه على ما عليه الحيلة ولا يقال كيف اكتفى هذا بالظاهر في سلامة أطرافه حتى جاز التكفير ولم يكتف بالظاهر في حد وجوب الضمان بإتلاف أطرافه لأنا نقول : الحاجة في التكفير إلى دفع الواجب والظاهر يصلح حجة للدافع والحاجة في الإتلاف إلى دفع الضمان لا وهو يصلح حجة فيه ولأنه يظهر حال الأطراف فيما بعد التكفير إذا عاش ولا كذلك الإتلاف فافترقا
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'And feeding and the fetus are not permissible' because feeding is not mentioned in the text [Qur'an], and the [fixed] quantities are not known except through hearing [revelation]; and because that which is mentioned [in the Qur'an] is the entire obligation, either in the answer or because it is the entirety of what is mentioned. And the fetus: its life and safety are not known, so it is not permissible; and because it is a limb from one aspect, so it does not fall under the absolute text. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'And the suckling infant is permissible if one of his parents is Muslim' because he is Muslim by following [them], and the apparent condition is the safety of his limbs according to the usual practice (على ما عليه الحيلة). And it should not be said: 'How did he suffice with the apparent condition for the safety of his limbs to permit expiation, yet he did not suffice with the apparent condition in the case of fixing liability for compensation for destroying limbs?' Because we say: In expiation, the need is to discharge the obligation, and the apparent condition is a valid proof for the discharger; while in the case of destruction, the need is to avoid liability, and the apparent condition is not a valid proof therein. And because the condition of the limbs will become apparent after the expiation if [the infant] lives, unlike the case of destruction, so they differ.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage discusses the specific conditions for the slave to be valid for kaffarah. First, it rules that 'feeding' (إطعام) is not valid as a kaffarah because the Quran specifically mentions freeing a 'slave' (رقبة), not feeding. The quantities (of expiation) are known only through revelation, and feeding is not mentioned. Second, a fetus is not valid because its life and safety are uncertain, and it is only a limb in some sense, so it does not meet the absolute requirement of a 'slave'. Third, a suckling infant (رضيع) is valid for kaffarah if one of his parents is Muslim, because he follows them in Islam, and the apparent condition (ghalabat al-zann) is that his limbs are intact. The author addresses a potential objection: why accept the apparent condition for expiation but not for liability when destroying limbs? The answer is that in expiation, the person is trying to discharge an obligation, and the apparent condition suffices; in the case of causing injury, the apparent condition does not suffice for avoiding liability because the need is to prove harm. Moreover, the condition of the limbs will later become apparent as the child grows, whereas in injury, it is immediate.

Key Terms
رضيع (radi'): A suckling infant; in this context, a child still nursing, considered valid for kaffarah if one parent is Muslim.
ظاهر (zahir): Apparent condition; here used to mean 'presumption' (ghalabat al-zann) about the infant's soundness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is a suckling infant valid for kaffarah?
A) If both parents are Muslims
B) If one parent is Muslim
C) Always
D) Never

Key Takeaway
In kaffarah, a suckling infant is valid for freeing if one parent is Muslim, relying on the apparent condition of sound limbs, unlike in liability cases where certainty is required.

No Compensation for a Wound That Heals Without Trace
Ruling with intra-Hanafi disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions on compensation for a wound that heals without trace, explain the proof of each imam, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A perfect healing leaves no trace and no compensation in Abu Hanifa's view; the Sahibayn disagree on residual harm.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a wound that heals completely without leaving any trace. The central question is whether compensation (arsh) is due when the visible blemish and loss of benefit have disappeared. Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani each offer a distinct position, reflecting different assessments of what constitutes compensable harm. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze liability in tort and the role of pain and consequential expenses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the legal question? A person is wounded; the wound heals and leaves no trace. Does the offender owe any compensation?
The wound is complete (برأ) and its effect (أثر) is gone.
2
State each Hanafi imam's position
Abu Hanifa: No compensation. Abu Yusuf: Compensation for pain (أرش الألم) assessed equitably. Muhammad: Doctor's fee (أجرة الطبيب).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; all three are within the school.
3
Trace each imam's proof
Abu Hanifa: The compensable factor is blemish and loss of benefit, both gone. Benefits are only compensable by contract, not tort. Mere pain is not compensable. He analogizes to a painful blow without wound. Abu Yusuf: Pain is a separate harm remaining after healing, so it merits compensation. Muhammad: The doctor's fee is a direct effect of the injury, therefore recoverable.
The text also mentions Sharh al-Tahawi reconciling Abu Yusuf's view with Muhammad's.
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, no payment. Under Abu Yusuf, the victim gets compensation for pain. Under Muhammad, the victim recovers medical expenses.
The difference affects actual liability in court.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) because it is from Zahir al-Riwayah and supported by the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq.
Thus the operative ruling is: no compensation for a wound that heals without trace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شج رجلاً فالتحم ولم يبق له أثر أو ضرب فجرح فبرأ وذهب أثره فلا أرش) وهذا قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : عليه أرش الألم وهو حكومة عدل لأن الشين الموجب إن زال فالألم الحاصل لم يزل. وقال محمد رحمه الله : عليه أجرة الطبيب لأن ذلك أثر فعله فكان له أخذ ذلك واعطاؤه الطبيب، وفي شرح الطحاوي فسر قول أبي يوسف عليه أرش الألم بأجرة الطبيب والمداواة فعلى هذا الاختلاف بين أبي يوسف ومحمد ولأبي حنيفة رحمه الله أن الموجب هو الشين الذي يلحقه بفعله وزوال منفعته وقد زال ذلك بزوال أثره والمنافع لا تتقوم إلا بالعقد ذلك كالإجارة والمضاربة الصحيحين أو ما يشبه العقد كالفاسد منهما ولم يوجد شيء من في حق الجاني فلا يلزم الغرامة، وكذلك مجرد الألم لا يوجب شيئاً لأنه لا قيمة له بمجرد الألم ألا ترى أن من ضرب إنساناً ضرباً مؤلماً من غير جرح لا يجب عليه شيء من الأرش، وكذا لأن لو شتمه شتماً يؤلم نفسه لا يضمن شيئاً.
And if he wounds a man and it heals and no trace remains, or strikes and cuts and it heals and its effect goes away, then no compensation (arsh). This is the statement of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Upon him is the compensation for the pain, which is an equitable assessment (hukumat 'adl), because the blemish that necessitated [compensation] has gone away, but the pain that occurred has not gone away. And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Upon him is the wage of the doctor, because that is an effect of his action, so he may take that and give it to the doctor. And in Sharh al-Tahawi, the statement of Abu Yusuf 'upon him is the compensation for pain' is interpreted as the wage of the doctor and medication. On this basis, the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is apparent. And for Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him: The causative factor is the blemish that he causes by his action and the loss of benefit, and that has disappeared with the disappearance of its trace, and benefits are not compensable except by contract, like valid contracts of hire (ijara) and mudaraba, or something resembling a contract like the invalid ones (fasid) of both. And nothing of that exists on the part of the offender, so he is not liable for indemnity. Similarly, mere pain does not necessitate anything because there is no value for mere pain. Do you not see that if someone strikes a person a painful blow without wounding, nothing of compensation is obligatory upon him? And likewise, if he insults him with an insult that pains his soul, he does not guarantee anything.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the liability for a wound that heals leaving no permanent trace. Abu Hanifa holds that no compensation (arsh) is due because the basis for liability—visible blemish and loss of benefit—has ceased. He argues that benefits are only compensable through contracts (like hire or mudaraba) or quasi-contracts, not through torts where the harm has been fully remedied. Mere pain without lasting effect does not warrant monetary compensation, analogously to a painful but non-wounding strike or a hurtful insult. Abu Yusuf differs, requiring compensation for pain (arsh al-alam) assessed by equitable estimation. Muhammad al-Shaybani requires payment of the doctor's fee, as it is a direct consequence of the injury. The commentary in Sharh al-Tahawi reconciles Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's views by interpreting 'arsh al-alam' as the doctor's fee. Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) as it is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation for physical injury short of homicide, measured by specified amounts or equitable assessment.
شين (shayn): Blemish or visible defect caused by injury, which is the basis for compensation.
الحكومة العدل (hukumat al-'adl): Equitable assessment by the judge of compensation for harm not covered by fixed arsh amounts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a wound that heals without leaving any trace?
A) The offender must compensate for pain assessed equitably.
B) The offender must pay the doctor's fee.
C) No compensation is due.
D) The offender must pay both pain compensation and doctor's fee.

Key Takeaway
For a wound that heals completely without trace, the Hanafi mufta bihi is no compensation; pain and medical expenses are not recoverable.

No Qisas for Wound Until Healed
Ruling that qisas for a wound is delayed until healing, with Shafi'i opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the timing of qisas for wounds and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument using the hadith and the rational proof of uncertain outcome.

Core Idea
Qisas for a wound is deferred until healing because the outcome is uncertain—it may become homicide.

In this passage, we examine a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on when qisas (retaliation) for a wound can be carried out. The Hanafi school insists on waiting until the wound heals to ensure it is not fatal, while the Shafi'i school argues for immediate execution by analogy with homicide. Understanding this debate illuminates how textual evidence and rational reasoning interact in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
Determine the ruling stated by the author (Imam al-Kanz): 'No qisas for a wound until it heals.' This is the base rule.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
State al-Shafi'i's view: qisas should be immediate because the cause (intentional harm) is established, just like homicide.
This is an analogy (qiyas) from homicide.
3
Analyze the Hanafi textual proof
Examine the hadith: the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade retaliation for a wound until healing. This is a direct prohibition (nahy) that overrides analogy.
Hadith from Ahmad and al-Daraqutni: 'نهى أن يقتص من جرح حتى يبرأ صاحبه'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rational proof
Consider the rational argument: wounds may become fatal, so the true nature (wound vs. homicide) is only known after healing. Immediate qisas risks punishing for a wound that turns out to be murder.
This is a precaution against uncertainty (shubha).
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical difference: Hanafi law postpones qisas execution; Shafi'i allows it immediately.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ولا قود بجرح حتى يبرأ) وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : يقتص منه في الحال لأن الموجب قد تحقق فلا يؤخر كما في القصاص في النفس ولنا ما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى أن يقتص من جرح حتى يبرأ صاحبه . رواه أحمد والدارقطني. ولأن الجراحات يعتبر فيها مآلهما لاحتمال أن تسري إلى النفس فيظهر أنه قتل فلا يعلم أنه جرح إلا بالبرء فيستقر به
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: And no qisas (retaliation) for a wound until it heals. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: He is retaliated against immediately, because the cause (of qisas) is realized, so it should not be delayed, just as in qisas for life. And for us (Hanafis): It is narrated that he (peace and blessings be upon him) forbade retaliation for a wound until its owner heals. Related by Ahmad and al-Daraqutni. And because for wounds, their outcomes are considered, due to the possibility that they may become fatal; then it becomes apparent that it was homicide, so it is not known that it is a wound until healing, and then qisas is confirmed.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage addresses a rule regarding qisas (retaliation) for wounds. Imam al-Kanz (the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states that no qisas is executed for a wound until the victim has healed. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that retaliation should be immediate because the just cause is established, similar to retaliation for murder. The Hanafi defense rests on two proofs: a hadith reported by Ahmad and al-Daraqutni forbidding retaliation for a wound until recovery, and a rational consideration that wounds may become fatal, so the true nature (whether it is a wound or homicide) is only known after healing. Thus, qisas is postponed to avoid the possibility of retaliating for a wound that later proves fatal, which would be misdirected. The hadith is the primary textual evidence, and the rational argument reinforces it. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
قود (qawad): Retaliation in kind for bodily injury or homicide; lex talionis.
برء (bur'): Healing of a wound, indicating that it is not fatal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary textual evidence for the Hanafi position that qisas for a wound is delayed until healing?
A) Analogy with qisas for murder.
B) A hadith forbidding retaliation for a wound until healing.
C) Consensus of the Companions.
D) The principle of legal certainty.

Key Takeaway
Qisas for a wound must be delayed until healing is confirmed, based on explicit prophetic prohibition and the uncertainty of wound outcomes.

Diyah for Intentional Killing Dropped Due to Shubha
Hanafi ruling on diyah liability and timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah for intentional killing when qisas is dropped due to shubha and identify who bears the liability and the timing of payment.

Core Idea
When qisas falls due to shubha, the killer pays full diyah from his own property — the 'aqila does not pay.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a critical distinction in Hanafi criminal law. While the 'aqila (tribal heirs) normally bear diyah for accidental killings, they are exempt in cases of intentional killing even when qisas is dropped due to a legal doubt (shubha). The killer must pay from his own wealth, and the timing of payment varies based on the basis of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Recognize that qisas is dropped due to a shubha, e.g., a father killing his son. This triggers the diyah ruling.
Shubha can be in the act or in the person.
2
State the ruling
Full diyah is wajib from the killer's personal property (mal al-qatil), not from the 'aqila.
The killer's property, not the clan's.
3
Cite the evidence
The hadith from Ibn Abbas: 'The 'aqila do not bear responsibility for intentional killing, nor for a slave, nor for settlement, nor for confession.'
This is a zanni al-thubut hadith but qati al-dalala in meaning.
4
Explain the rationale
The 'aqila's liability is a leniency for the mistaken killer; an intentional killer warrants severity, so the burden stays on him.
Also, settlement is a contract, and confession binds only the confessor.
5
Determine payment timing
Generally, diyah is deferred over three years. Exception: payment due by settlement is immediate; wounds below one-third diyah are paid in one year.
Small amounts are immediate to avoid unnecessary delay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وكل عمد سقط فيه قوده لشبهة كقتل الأب ابنه عمداً ففيه دية في مال القاتل وكذا ما صلحاً أو اعترافاً أو لم يكن نصف العشر) لما روي عن ابن عباس موقوفاً ومرفوعاً لا تعقل العاقلة عمداً ولا عبداً ولا صلحاً ولا اعترافاً ولأن العاقلة تتحمل عن القاتل تخفيفاً . وذلك يليق بالمخطئ لأنه معذور دون المتعمد لأنه يوجب التغليظ والذي وجب بالصلح إنما وجب بعقده والعاقلة لا تتحمل ما يجب العقد وإنما تتحمل ما يجب بالقتل، وكذا ما لزمه بالإقرار لا تتحمله العاقلة لأن له ولاية على نفسه دون عاقلته فيلزمه دونهم. وإنما لا تتحمل أقل من نصف عشر الدية لأنه لا يؤدي إلى الإجحاف والاستئصال بالجاني والتأجيل تحرزاً عنه فلا حاجة إليه، ثم الكل يجب مؤجلاً إلى ثلاث سنين إلا ما وجب بالصلح فإنه يجب حالاً لأنه واجب بالعقد فيكون حالاً بخلاف غيره وما دونه أرش الموضحة يجب في سنة لا ما دون ثلث الدية والثلث وما دونه يجب في سنة.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And every intentional [killing] in which qisas is dropped due to a doubt (shubha), like a father intentionally killing his son, then in it is diyah (blood money) from the property of the killer. And likewise what [is due] by settlement (sulh) or by confession (iqrar), or if it was not half of a tenth [of the full diyah].) Because it is narrated from Ibn Abbas as a mawquf and marfu' [report]: The 'aqila (tribal heirs) do not bear [responsibility] for intentional killing, nor for a slave, nor for settlement, nor for confession. And because the 'aqila bear on behalf of the killer as a leniency. That is appropriate for the mistaken [killer] because he is excused, unlike the intentional [killer] because it necessitates severity. And what becomes obligatory by settlement is only obligatory by contract, and the 'aqila do not bear what is obligatory by contract; they bear what is obligatory by killing. Likewise, what is necessitated by confession, the 'aqila do not bear it, because he has authority over himself [to confess] but not over his 'aqila, so it is binding on him alone. And they do not bear less than half of a tenth of the diyah, because it does not lead to oppression and destruction of the offender, and deferment is precautionary, so there is no need for it. Then all [of the above] is payable in installments over three years, except what is obligatory by settlement, for it is due immediately because it is obligatory by contract, so it is immediate, unlike others. And what is less than the arsh of al-mudiha [a specified wound] is due in one year, not less than one-third of the diyah, and one-third and what is less is due in one year.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage explains the liability for diyah when qisas for intentional killing is dropped due to a shubha (doubt), such as a father killing his son. In such cases, the full diyah is due from the killer's personal property (mal al-qatil), not from the 'aqila (tribal or clan heirs who normally pay diyah for accidental killing). The same rule applies if the liability arises by settlement (sulh) or by confession of the killer, or if the due amount is less than half of one-tenth of the full diyah (i.e., less than 1/20 of the full diyah, which is 5 camels out of 100). The evidence is a hadith from Ibn Abbas that the 'aqila do not bear responsibility for 'amd (intentional killing), nor for a slave, nor for settlements, nor for confessions. The reasoning is that the 'aqila's liability is a concession for the mistaken killer, not for the intentional one. Settlement is a contract, and the 'aqila do not cover contractual debts. Confession is binding only on the confessor, not on his 'aqila. For amounts below half a tenth, the 'aqila also do not pay because they are insignificant. Payment is deferred over three years for all these cases, except for settlement, which is due immediately like any contractual debt. For the arsh of mudiha (a specific head wound), the payment period is one year if the amount is less than one-third of the full diyah.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aqila): The male clansmen or tribe responsible for paying diyah on behalf of a killer in cases of accidental or quasi-intentional killing.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of qisas but still entails diyah (e.g., a father killing his son).
صلح (sulh): A settlement agreement between the victim's heirs and the killer regarding diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the passage, which of the following is NOT a category for which the 'aqila bears liability?
A) Intentional killing ('amd)
B) Accidental killing (khatā')
C) Killing a slave
D) Settlement (sulh)

Key Takeaway
When qisas is dropped due to shubha, diyah is wajib from the killer's own property, paid over three years (immediate if by settlement), and the 'aqila is not liable.

Timing of Payment for Diyah: Default, Settlement, and Arsh Cases
Ruling on timing of diyah payment with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three timing rules for diyah payment according to the Hanafi school and apply them to scenarios involving different amounts and bases.

Core Idea
Diyah is generally paid over three years, but contractual settlement requires immediate payment, and smaller wounds are paid in one year.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies exactly when a diyah payment falls due. While the standard rule is a three-year deferment, there are two important exceptions: diyah agreed by settlement must be paid at once, and the arsh for wounds like al-mudiha is payable in one year if it does not exceed one-third of the full diyah. Understanding these distinctions prevents confusion when applying the law to real cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
Locate the phrase 'الكل يجب مؤجلاً إلى ثلاث سنين' — all diyah is deferred to three years. This includes diyah for 'amd shibh, confession, and amounts below half a tenth.
The default is deferment, not immediacy.
2
Isolate the exception for settlement
Find 'إلا ما وجب بالصلح فإنه يجب حالاً'. Diyah arising from sulh is treated as a contractual debt and must be paid immediately.
Sulh changes the nature from tort to contract.
3
Determine the rule for arsh below one-third
The text says 'وما دونه أرش الموضحة يجب في سنة لا ما دون ثلث الدية والثلث وما دونه يجب في سنة'. The arsh of mudiha (and any amount less than one-third of diyah) is due in one year.
One-third and less are time-wise the same; more than one-third reverts to three years.
4
Apply the rules to a scenario
Given a case where diyah is set at 50 camels via sulh, the payment is immediate. If the arsh of mudiha is 5 camels (one-twentieth), it is paid in one year. For any diyah above one-third, e.g., 100 camels, pay over three years.
The key variable is the amount relative to one-third and the basis (sulh vs. non-sulh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الكل يجب مؤجلاً إلى ثلاث سنين إلا ما وجب بالصلح فإنه يجب حالاً لأنه واجب بالعقد فيكون حالاً بخلاف غيره وما دونه أرش الموضحة يجب في سنة لا ما دون ثلث الدية والثلث وما دونه يجب في سنة.
Then all is obligatory deferred to three years, except what is obligatory by settlement (sulh), for it is obligatory immediately because it is obligatory by contract, so it is immediate, unlike others. And what is less than the arsh of al-mudiha (a specific head wound) is obligatory in one year, not less than one-third of the diyah, and one-third and what is less is obligatory in one year.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This short passage specifies the timing for paying diyah in different situations. The default rule: all diyah (including that from 'amd with shubha, confession, and amounts below half a tenth) is payable in installments over three years. Exception: if the diyah is determined by a settlement contract (sulh), it must be paid immediately because it is a contractual debt, which is not subject to deferment. For the arsh of mudiha (a specific wound exposing bone), if it is less than one-third of the full diyah, the payment period is one year. The rule also applies when the amount is exactly one-third or less. This is part of the Hanafi rules on timing of diyah payment.

Key Terms
الموضحة (al-mudiha): A specific head wound that exposes the bone, for which a fixed arsh is prescribed.
مؤجل (mu'ajjal): Deferred payment, typically over three years for diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is diyah paid if it is established by a settlement (sulh)?
A) Deferred over three years
B) Deferred over one year
C) Paid immediately
D) Paid in installments according to the killer's ability

Key Takeaway
Diyah payment timing depends on the basis: default three years, contractual settlement immediate, arsh ≤ one-third one year.

Intentional Act of Minor and Insane is Treated as Accidental (Khata')
Ruling that the intentional act of a minor or insane person is treated as accidental for diyah purposes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school treats the intentional killing by a minor or insane person as accidental (khata') and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The intentional act of a minor or insane person is legally treated as accidental because they lack the capacity for criminal intent.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a key Hanafi doctrine in the law of homicide: the intentional killing by a minor or insane person is legally classified as khata' (mistake), not 'amd (intentional). This classification carries significant consequences for diyah, kaffarah, and inheritance. The text also presents and refutes the Shafi'i position. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school hold regarding the intentional act of a minor or insane person?
Note the three consequences: diyah on 'aqila, no kaffarah, no disinheritance.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
What does Imam al-Shafi'i argue?
He argues that the act is truly intentional, so diyah should be from the perpetrator's property.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
What is their dalil? They analogize to theft and point to discipline (ta'dib) and ta'zir.
Look for the analogy with theft and the argument about capacity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut? They cite the precedent of 'Ali (RA), the hadith on mercy, and the analogy with a sleeping person.
Key points: lack of reason prevents genuine intent; they are deserving of leniency.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in financial liability and expiation?
Hanafi: liability on 'aqila; Shafi'i: liability on the perpetrator's own property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : ( وعمد الصبي والمجنون خطأ وديته على عاقلته ولا تكفير فيه ولا حرمان فيه) أي عن الميراث والمعتوه كالصبي. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : عمده عمد فتجب الدية في القصد وهو ضد الخطأ فمن يتحقق منه الخطأ يتحقق منه العمد ولهذا يؤدب ويعزر، وكان ينبغي أن يجب القصاص إلا أنه سقط للشبهة لأنهم ليسوا من أهل العقوبة فيجب عليهم موجبه الآخر وهو المال لأنهم أهل لوجوبه عليهم فصار نظير السرقة فإنهم إذا سرقوا لا يقطع أيديهم ويجب عليهم ضمان المال المسروق لما قلنا، ولهذا وجب عليهم التكفير بالمال لأنه أهل لفوات المالية دون الصوم لعدم الخطاب، وكذا يحرم الميراث عنده بالقتل. ولنا أن مجنوناً صال على رجل بسيف فضربه فرفع ذلك إلى علي رضي الله فجعل عقله على عاقلته بمحضر من الصحابة رضي الله وقال : عمده وخطؤه سواء. ولأن الصبي مظنة المرحمة قال عليه الصلاة والسلام من لم يرحم صغيرنا ولم يوقر كبيرنا فليس منا» (1) والعاقل المخطئ لما استحق التخفيف حتى وجبت الدية على عاقلته فهؤلاء أولى بهذا التخفيف فيجب على العاقلة إذا كان الواجب قدر نصف العشر أو أكثر بخلاف ما دونه لأنه يسلك به مسلك الأموال كما في البالغ العاقل لأنه لم يتحقق العمد منه لأنه عبارة عن وهو يترتب على العلم والعلم بالعقل. وهؤلاء عدموا العقل فكيف يتحقق منهم القصد وصاروا كالنائم، وحرمان الإرث عقوبة وهم ليسوا من أهلها، والكفارة كاسمها ساترة ولا ذنب لهم تستره لأنهم مرفوع عنهم القلم، ولأن الكفارة دائرة بين العبارة والعقوبة يعني أن فيها معنى العبادة ومعنى العقوبة ولا يجب عليهم عبادة ولا عقوبة، وكذا سبب الكفارة تكون دائرة بين الخطر والإباحة لكون العقوبة متعلقة بالحظر وفعلهم لا يوصف بالجناية لأنها اسم لفعل محظور وكل ذلك ينبئ عن الخطاب وهم ليسوا بمخاطبين فكيف تجب عليهم الكفارة والله أعلم .
He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him: (And the intentional [killing] of a minor (sabi) and an insane person (majnun) is [treated as] accidental (khata'), and its diyah is upon his 'aqila, and there is no kaffarah (expiation) in it, and no deprivation (of inheritance) in it.) – meaning from inheritance, and the dim-witted (ma'tuh) is like the minor. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Their intentional act is intentional, so the diyah is obligatory due to intent, which is opposite of mistake; so whoever is verified to have made a mistake can also be verified to have intent. For this reason they are disciplined and punished (ta'zir). Retaliation (qisas) should be obligatory, but it is dropped due to doubt (shubha) because they are not of those subject to punishment (ahl al-'uquba). So the other consequence is obligatory upon them, which is property (mal) because they are of those obligated to [pay] it. So it becomes like theft: when they steal, their hands are not cut but they must pay the guarantee (daman) for the stolen property, as we said. For this reason, kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory upon them in property because they are capable of losing wealth, unlike fasting (sawm) due to lack of address (khitab). Likewise, he holds that they are deprived of inheritance by killing. And for us (Hanafis): An insane person attacked a man with a sword and struck him, and that was raised to 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and he placed his 'aql [i.e., responsibility] upon his 'aqila in the presence of the Companions, and he said: 'His intentional and his mistake are the same.' And because the minor is deserving of mercy. He said, peace be upon him: 'Whoever does not be merciful to our young and does not respect our old is not of us.' And the sane mistaken [killer] deserved leniency such that the diyah is upon his 'aqila, so these [minors/insane] are more deserving of this leniency. So it is obligatory upon the 'aqila if what is obligatory is half of a tenth or more, contrary to what is less than that, because it is treated like property matters, just as in the case of a sane adult, because intentionality is not verified from them, since it (intent) depends on knowledge and knowledge depends on reason. And these [minors/insane] lack reason, so how can intent be realized from them? They become like a sleeping person. And deprivation of inheritance is a punishment, and they are not of those who deserve punishment. And kaffarah, as its name suggests, is a concealment, and they have no sin to conceal, because the pen is lifted from them. And because kaffarah fluctuates between [terms of] expression and punishment – meaning it contains an element of worship and an element of punishment – and neither worship nor punishment is obligatory upon them. Likewise, the cause of kaffarah fluctuates between prohibition (hazr) and permissibility (ibaha), because punishment is associated with prohibition, and their act is not described as a crime (jinaya) because that is a name for a prohibited act. And all of that indicates [requirement of] address (khitab), and they are not addressed, so how can kaffarah be obligatory upon them? And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This lengthy passage addresses the legal treatment of intentional killing (or injury) by a minor (sabi) or an insane person (majnun). The Hanafi position, stated by the author, is that their 'amd (intentional act) is legally treated as khata' (mistake). Consequently, the diyah is payable by the 'aqila (tribal heirs), not by the perpetrator's own property. Additionally, no kaffarah (expiation) is required, and the perpetrator is not deprived of inheritance if they were the killer (contrary to the general rule that a killer does not inherit). The same ruling applies to the ma'tuh (feeble-minded person). Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that their intentional act is truly intentional; therefore, diyah should be based on intent, not treated as mistake. He claims that since they are liable for discipline (ta'dib) and discretionary punishment (ta'zir), they demonstrate capacity for intent. Qisas would be due except it is dropped due to shubha (doubt about their legal capacity), so the alternative (diyah) is due. He analogizes to theft: they do not have their hands cut but must guarantee the property. He also argues for kaffarah (in property, not fasting) and deprivation of inheritance. The Hanafi response relies on a precedent from 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA) who ruled on an insane attacker, making diyah on his 'aqila and stating: 'His 'amd and his khata' are the same.' This equates their act to mistake. The Hanafis also cite a hadith about mercy to the young, indicating that minors are deserving of leniency. Since a sane mistaken killer already benefits from leniency (diyah on 'aqila), minors and insane persons are even more entitled to that leniency. The Hanafi reasoning further argues that genuine intent (qasd) requires knowledge which requires reason (aql); lacking reason, they cannot form intent, so their act is akin to an involuntary act (like a sleeping person). Deprivation of inheritance is a punishment not applicable to those without culpability. Kaffarah contains elements of worship and punishment; since they are not addressed by legal obligations (raf' al-qalam), kaffarah is not required.

Key Terms
خطأ (khata'): Legal mistake (accidental); the killer's act is not intentional or quasi-intentional; diyah on 'aqila.
تكفير (kaffarah): Expiation for accidental killing: freeing a slave or fasting two months.
حرمان (hirman): Deprivation of inheritance for a killer in certain circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of intentional killing by a minor according to the Hanafi school?
A) Qisas is applied because it was intentional.
B) The minor pays diyah from his own property.
C) Diyah is on the 'aqila, no kaffarah, no disinheritance.
D) The minor is executed after reaching puberty.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the intentional act of a minor or insane person is treated as accidental, resulting in diyah on the 'aqila, no kaffarah, and no disinheritance.

Introduction to the Section on Foetal Injuries (Jinayat al-Janin)
Section heading introducing foetal injury rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a Hanafi fiqh text transitions from general rules of jinayat to specific rules for the foetus and state the legal significance of this distinction.

Core Idea
The foetus is a human from one aspect but not another, requiring unique rulings.

In the study of Hanafi fiqh, section headings (fusul) are not mere decorations—they signal a shift in subject matter and often introduce a new set of principles. Here, after discussing injuries to a fully developed human (adami), the author Ibn Nujaym turns to the foetus (janin). This transition is crucial because the foetus, while not yet a complete legal person in all respects, holds a protected status under Shari'a, and causing its loss incurs specific liabilities.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structural cue
The phrase 'فصل في الجنين' marks a new sub-section. Note that the preceding section dealt with jinayat on a fully born human. Identify this as a topic shift.
In classical fiqh works, 'fasl' often indicates a narrower focus within a larger bab (chapter).
2
Understand the author's rationale
The author states he begins explaining rulings 'attached to a human from one aspect but not another.' This refers to the foetus being a human in potential and deserving of protection, but lacking full legal independence.
The phrase 'من وجه دون وجه' is key: the foetus shares some human attributes (e.g., life after 120 days) but not others (e.g., independent existence).
3
Appreciate the legal implications
Because of this dual status, the diyah (blood money) for a foetus differs from that of a born human, and certain conditions apply. This section will detail those rules.
For example, the diyah for a miscarried foetus is a ghurrah (a slave or 500 dirhams) if the foetus was beyond 120 days.

Source Text

لما ذكر أحكام الجناية المتعلقة بالآدمي شرع في بيان أحكامها المعلقة بالآدمي من وجه دون
Section on the foetus (janin). Since he mentioned the rulings of injury related to a full human being (adami), he began to explain its rulings that are attached to a human being from one aspect but not another.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This is a section heading (fasl) introducing the topic of injuries affecting the foetus. The author Ibn Nujaym transitions from the general rules of jinayat (injuries) on a fully developed human (adami) to the specific cases involving a foetus (janin). The fiqh of the foetus deals with two main scenarios: (1) a direct injury to a pregnant woman causing miscarriage, and (2) an injury directly to the foetus itself. The Hanafi school has detailed rulings on the diyah for a foetus at various stages of development, and the conditions for liability. The heading indicates that the following text will elaborate on these rules, although the current passage only gives the introduction.

Key Terms
جنين (janin): A foetus; an unborn human in the womb.
آدمي (adami): A full human being; a born, independent person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The section on the foetus (janin) begins by highlighting its unique legal status, distinct from a fully born human, which forms the basis for specific jinayat rulings.
