10 min
Slave's Report and Confession (Iqrar) Scenarios
Rulings on slave's report and confession scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when the slave informs the master of the crime and the master reacts, and analyze the complex confession scenarios involving multiple parties.

Core Idea
The master's confession of the injury and ownership of the slave can create or deny the option, depending on the confessee's response.

This lesson covers two final topics: the effect of the slave's own report of the injury (and the dispute between Abu Hanifa and his disciples on the required reliability of the informant), and the intricate rules of iqrar (confession) when the master acknowledges the injury and/or that the slave belongs to another. These scenarios determine who bears the liability and whether the master has the option.

Guided Path
1
Understand the slave's informing scenario
If the slave tells his master about the injury and the master then emancipates the slave, claiming he did not believe the slave, Abu Hanifa requires a just free witness to confirm; the disciples (Sahibayn) accept even a sinful or non-Muslim informant.
This affects whether the master is liable for diya.
2
Classify the confession cases
The text categorizes iqrar by order: (1) confesses injury then ownership to another, or (2) ownership then injury. Also by whether the master's ownership is known or unknown.
The key is whether the master ends up with the option or the liability shifts.
3
Trace the case where the master confesses injury first, then claims ownership for another, and his ownership is known
If the confessee (the one named as owner) confirms both the injury and ownership, the confessee becomes the party responsible (must hand over or ransom). If the confessee denies only the injury, the master does not become mukhtar (contrary to Zufar's view).
This shows that the option is tied to valid iqrar.
4
Trace the case where the master confesses ownership first, then injury
If the confessee confirms both, the confessee (as owner) becomes liable. If the confessee confirms ownership but denies injury, the injury is not established (it becomes baseless). If he denies both, the master remains the defendant.
Also consider when ownership is unknown (by mere possession).
5
Consider the final scenario: master says he sold the slave before the injury
If the master states he sold the slave before the injury and the buyer confirms, the buyer becomes the one with the option (he has the choice to hand over or ransom).
This shows that the option follows ownership at the time of the injury.

كتاب الديات باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه
الحال مختاراً للزم . منه ملك غيره وهنا لا يلزم ، ولأنه يلزم في البيع بيع الغرر هنا لا يلزم. ولو باعه بيعاً فاسداً . لم يصير مختاراً للفداء حتى يسلمه لأن الملك لا يزول إلا به بخلاف الكتابة الفاسدة حيث يكون مختاراً للفداء لأن حكم الكتابة تعلق العتق بأداء المال وفك الحجر عن العبد في وهو ثابت بنفس الكتابة ولا كذلك البيع الفاسد لأن حكمه وهو الملك لا يثبت إلا بالقبض. ولو كانت الكتابة صحيحة ثم عجز كان له أن يدفعه بالجناية، فإن كان ذلك قبل أن يقضي عليه بالقيمة وبعدها لا يدفعه لتقرر القيمة بالقضاء ولو باعه من المجني عليه كان مختاراً للفداء بخلاف ما إذا وهبه منه لأن المستحق له أخذه بغير عوض وهو متحقق في الهبة دون البيع واعتاق المجني عليه بأمر المولى بمنزلة إعتاق المولى فيما ذكرنا لأن فعل المأمور به ينتقل إلى الآمر . ولو ضربه فنقصه كان مختاراً بعد العلم لأنه جنس جزء منه، فإن أزال النقصان قبل القضاء بالقيمة كان له أن يدفعه بها لزوال المانع من الدفع قبل استقرار القيمة ويصير بالإجازة والرهن في رواية كتاب الإعتاق لأنهما لازمان فيكون محدثاً فيه ما يعجز عن الدفع والأظهر أنه لا يصير مختاراً بهما للفداء لأنه لم يعجزه عن الدفع لأن له أن يفسخ الإجازة والرهن لحق المجني لتعلق حقه بعين العبد سابقاً على حقهما فيفسخان صوناً لحقه . عن البطلان، وكذا لا مختاراً بالإذن في التجارة وإن ركبه دين لأن الإذن لا يفوت الدفع ولا ينقض الرقبة إلا أن يصير مختاراً لمولى الجناية أن يمتنع من القبول لأن الدين لحقه من جهة المولى بعدما تعلق به حقه فلزم المولى قيمته. ولو جنى جنايتين فعلم بأحدهما دون الأخرى وتصرف به تصرفاً يصير به تصرفاً مختاراً للفداء فيما ،علم وفيما لا يعلم يلزمه حصته من قيمة العبد . وقوله كبيعه وتعليق عتقه بقتل فلان أو رميه وشجه إن فعل ذلك أي يصير مختاراً ببيعه بعد العلم بها وبتعليق عتقه بما ذكرنا من القتل والرمي والشج يصير مختاراً كما . مختاراً بالإعتاق بعد الإعلام بها، وإنما يصير . مختاراً بالتعليق عند علمائنا الثلاثة . وقال زفر : لا يصير مختاراً كما لا يصير مختاراً بالإعتاق بعد الإعلام بها وإنما يصير مختاراً بما ذكرنا لأن أوان تكلمه به لا جناية من العبد ولا علم للمولى بما سيوجد بعدو بعد الجناية لم يوجد منه فعل يصير به مختاراً ألا ترى أنه لو علق الطلاق أو العتاق بالشرط ثم حلف أن لا يطلق أو لا يعتق ثم وجد الشرط وثبت العتق والطلاق لا يحنث بذلك في يمينه فكذا هذا ولنا أنه علق الإعتاق بالجناية والمعلق بالشرط ينزل عند وجود الشرط كالمنجز عنده فصار كما إذا أعتقه بعد الجناية ألا ترى أن من قال لامرأته إذا دخلت الدار فوالله لا أقربك أربعة أشهر يصير ابتداء الإيلاء من وقت الدخول، وكذا إن قال لها إذا مرضت فأنت طالق ذلك ثلاثاً ومات من مطلقاً فاراً لأنه يصير يصير بعد الدخول ووجود المرض بخلاف ما أورده لأن غرضه طلاق أو عتاق يمكنه الامتناع عنه فلا يدخل تحته ما لا يمكنه الامتناع عنه، حرصه على مباشرة الشرط بتعليق أقوى الدواعي إلى القتل والظاهر أنه يفعله وهذا دلالة الاختيار هذا إذا علقه بجناية توجب المال كالخطأ وشبه العمد، وإن علقه بجناية توجب القصاص بأن قال له إن ضربته بالسيف فأنت حر فلا يجب على المولى شيء بالاتفاق لأنه لا فرق بين العبد والحر في القصاص فلم يكن المولى مفوتاً حق ولي الجناية بالعتق . وبكل قتل تجب الكفارة فيه يصير المولى مختاراً كالقتل بالمباشرة، وإن لم تجب الكفارة فيه لا مختاراً وهو القتل تسبباً كما لو وقع في بئر حفرها المولى لأن القتل تسبباً ليس بقتل حقيقة لأن القتل فعل في الحر ويؤثر في إزهاق الروح والتسبب ليس بفعل في الحر لأنه لم يوصل إلا إلى الدية ولهذا لم يجب القصاص ولا يحرم الإرث فلم يصر مستملكاً للعبد وبالقتل مباشرة صار مستدعياً للعمد في كل موضع صار متلفاً للعبد يضمن الفداء لما بينا. ولو أخبره عبده بالجناية فاعتقه المولى وقال لم أصدقه فعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لا يضمن ما لم يخبره رجل حر عدل، وعندهما يضمن الدية وإن كان المخبر فاسقاً أو كافراً وقد مرت في الوكالة والشفعة ولو به لغيره فهو على قسمين : إما أن أقر بالجناية أولاً ثم بالملك أو على عكسه، وكل قسم لا يخلوا إما أن كان الملك في العبد معروفاً للمقر أو كان مجهولاً. أما القسم الأول لو أقر بالجناية ثم بالملك لغيره والملك في العبد معروف للمقر ، فإن صدقة المقر له في الملك والجناية جميعاً يقال للمقر له ادفع العبد أو فده لأنه صح الإقرار لأن حق المجني عليه لا يمنع نفوذ تصرف المولى لأن حقه في الدافع أو الفداء وهو باق بعد الاقرار والثابت بالإقرار كالثابت بالبينة العادلة، ومتى ظهر الملك للمقر له بالإقرار ظهر أن الجناية صدرت من ملكه وإن كان كذبه فيها لا يكون المقر مختاراً للفداء خلافاً لزفر ؛ له أن صحة الإقرار لا تتوقف على تصديق المقر له، ولهذا لو مات المقر قبل التصديق يصير المقر به ميراثاً لورثته فقد زال العبد عن ملكه بنفس الإقرار وهو عالم بالجناية فيصير مختاراً. ولنا أن صحة الإقرار لا توجب على التصديق والبطلان يتوقف على التكذيب وإذا اتصل به التكذيب بطل من الأصل، فلو صدقه في الملك وكذبه في الجناية صار المقر مختاراً للفداء لأن الإقرار بالجناية على العبد صادف ملكه في العبد فصح، ثم إذا أقر بالملك لغيره وصدقه المقر له صار مزيلاً للعبد عن ملكه فصار كما لو باعه أو وهبه . وأما القسم الثاني لو أقر بالملك أولاً ثم بالجناية إن صدقه فيهما فالخصم هو المقر له، وإن كذبه فيهما فالخصم. المقر، وإن أنه للمقر أم لغيره فأقر بالجناية أولاً ثم بالملك أو بالملك أولاً ثم بالجناية لأن الملك ثابت للمقر بظاهر اليد لا يستند إلى دليل والملك الثابت بظاهر اليد لا يصلح حجة للاستحقاق واختيار الفداء فلم يصر مختاراً للفداء بخلاف ما لو كان الملك له معروفاً لأن ملكه ثابت مستند إلى دليل سوى ظاهر اليد فصلح حجة لاثبات ما لم يكن، ولو قال كنت بعته من فلان قبل الجناية وصدقه فلان يخير المشتري بين الدفع والفداء Book of Blood Money, Chapter on Injury of the Slave and Injury upon Him. The case: [the master] becomes one with choice (mukhtar) is obligatory [؟]. ... [full translation omitted for brevity] ...
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is a sharh (expansion) of Kanz al-Daqaiq covering the chapter on injuries involving slaves. The opening line continues a discussion about when a master becomes 'mukhtar' (having the option to ransom the slave or hand him over). The commentator explains various scenarios: a void sale does not make the master mukhtar until delivery, unlike a void kitaba (contract of manumission) where the master is immediately mukhtar because the legal effects (tied to payment) are established at contract. A valid kitaba followed by inability to pay allows the master to hand over the slave for the injury, but only before the court assesses the slave's value. Selling the slave to the victim makes the master mukhtar, but gifting does not because the victim takes without compensation in a gift, which already achieves the goal. If the master orders the victim to emancipate the slave, it is like the master emancipating. If the master injures the slave causing a defect, he becomes mukhtar after knowledge because he is reducing his own property; if he cures the defect before court valuation, the right to hand over is restored. Leasing or pawning the slave does not make the master mukhtar because he can rescind those contracts to preserve the victim's right. Permitting the slave to trade or incurring debt does not affect the option. If the slave commits two injuries, the master's knowledge of one makes him mukhtar for that one, and he must pay a share for the unknown. The matn (Kanz) mentions that sale and conditional emancipation (ta'liq) on killing, throwing, or wounding make the master mukhtar. The three Hanafi imams hold that conditional emancipation makes him mukhtar because the condition is treated as existent when fulfilled. Zufar disagrees, analogizing to a conditional oath where the condition does not make the oath false. The Hanafi response uses the principle that a conditional act is like an immediate act upon fulfillment, citing analogies from ila' (oath of abstinence) and divorce. Conditional emancipation only applies to injuries requiring monetary compensation (like khata' and shibh al-amd); if it triggers qisas (retaliation), the master owes nothing because the slave is like a free person in qisas. For killing that requires expiation (kafara), the master becomes mukhtar if by direct act; if by indirect causation (tasabbub), he does not, because indirect killing is not true killing—it only leads to diya without qisas and does not prevent inheritance. If the slave informs the master of the injury and the master emancipates while claiming disbelief, Abu Hanifa holds no liability unless a just free man confirms; the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold liability even if the informant is sinful or non-Muslim. The final portion deals with iqrar (confession) of the master: if he confesses to the injury then to the slave belonging to another, and the confessee confirms both, the confessee must hand over or ransom. If the confessee denies the injury, the confesor is not mukhtar because the confession of injury is invalidated by denial. If the confessee confirms ownership but denies injury, the injury is invalid. If the master's ownership is known only by apparent possession (yad), it is not sufficient for establishing the right to choose ransom. If the master says he sold the slave before the injury and the buyer confirms, the buyer chooses between handing over and ransoming.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Confession: a legal admission that creates rights or obligations. In this context, the master's admission of injury and/or ownership.
ظاهر اليد (zāhir al-yad): Apparent possession: mere physical control without proven ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab as presented in the text, what is the ruling when a master confesses first to the injury of his slave (ownership known) and then confesses that the slave belongs to someone else, and that person confirms ownership but denies the injury?
A) The master becomes mukhtar because the initial confession of injury was valid.
B) The master does not become mukhtar because the injury is not confirmed.
C) The confessee (the new owner) becomes responsible and must hand over or ransom.
D) The injury is invalid and no one is liable.

Key Takeaway
The slave's report creates liability only if corroborated by a just witness (Abu Hanifa) or any informant (Sahibayn); iqrar scenarios are complex and depend on the order of confession and the confessee's response.

Validity of Confession by Mudabbar and Umm Walad
Explains validity of mudabbar/umm walad confession for torts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a mudabbar or umm walad's confession to a tort is valid or invalid and distinguish between monetary torts and retaliation.

Core Idea
A slave's confession is valid only when it affects his own person (qisas), not when it imposes liability on his master.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi slave law: the validity of a mudabbar or umm walad's confession hinges on who bears the liability. As you read the Arabic, focus on the contrast between 'tort requiring monetary compensation' and 'tort requiring retaliation'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal capacity
Recognize that mudabbar and umm walad are slaves who will be freed upon the master's death. Their torts are generally on the master's account, but their confessions are treated differently based on the nature of the tort.
2
Distinguish the two types of torts
For torts requiring monetary compensation (e.g., accidental killing), liability is on the master, so the slave's confession is invalid. For torts requiring retaliation (intentional killing), the penalty is on the slave's own person, so confession is valid.
The governing principle: confession is effective only when it affects the confessor's own right, not another's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أقر المدبر وأم الولد بجناية توجب المال لم يجز إقراره وجنايته على المولى لا على نفسه وإقراره على المولى غير نافذ بخلاف ما إذا كانت الجناية موجبة للقود بأن أقر بالقتل عمدا حيث يصح إقراره فيقتل به لأن إقراره على نفسه فينفذ عليه لعدم التهمة
And if the mudabbar and the umm walad confess to a tort that necessitates monetary compensation, their confession is not valid; their tort is upon the master, not upon themselves, and their confession against the master is not effective, contrary to the case where the tort necessitates retaliation, such as when he confesses to deliberate killing, for then his confession is valid and he is killed for it because his confession is against himself and thus executed upon him due to the absence of suspicion.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding the legal effect of a mudabbar or umm walad's confession to a tort. It distinguishes between two types of torts: (1) Torts requiring monetary compensation (diyah): The confession is invalid because the liability falls on the master, not on the slave himself. The slave cannot impose liability on the master through confession. (2) Torts requiring retaliation (qisas): The confession is valid because the penalty (death) is executed on the slave's own person, not on the master. There is no suspicion of collusion because the slave is risking his own life. The same rule applies to umm walad (the mother of the master's child) because she too is the master's property until his death.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A confession or admission of a right or liability.
قود (qisas): Retaliation, specifically the death penalty for intentional killing.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death.
أم الولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne her master a child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a mudabbar's confession for a monetary tort invalid according to this passage?
A) Because the slave is not trustworthy.
B) Because the liability falls on the master, and the confession would impose liability on another.
C) Because the tort did not occur.
D) Because the master must confess on his behalf.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession is only binding on himself when the penalty is personal (qisas); it cannot bind the master for monetary liabilities.

Liability of a Mahjur Slave for Seizing Another Mahjur Slave
Commentary on the ruling when a legally incompetent slave seizes another like him and it dies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mahjur slave seizes another mahjur slave resulting in death and explain the Hanafi rationale based on tort liability.

Core Idea
A legally restricted (mahjur) slave is liable for tortious acts despite his incapacity for property transactions.

The Hanafi school maintains that even a person under legal incapacity (mahjur) is answerable for his harmful actions. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq applies that principle to a specific case: a mahjur slave who seizes another mahjur slave, causing its death, must compensate its value. We will unpack the ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The scenario involves two mahjur slaves: one usurps (seizes) the other. The seized slave dies while in the usurper's hand.
2
State the ruling
The usurping slave is liable (daman) and must compensate the value of the deceased slave.
3
Examine the rationale
The text states: 'because the mahjur is held accountable for his actions and this is among them.' This establishes general tort liability for mahjur persons.
4
Generalize the principle
Legal incapacity (hajr) for property management does not exempt from liability for harmful acts (torts). The same rule applies to minors and the insane in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محجور مثله فمات في يده ضمن) يعني إذا غصب عبد محجور عليه عبداً محجوراً عليه فمات المغصوب في يد الغاصب ضمنه لأن المحجور عليه مؤاخذ بأفعاله وهذا منها فيضمن.
A mahjur like him dies in his hand; he guarantees. Meaning: If a slave who is under legal restriction (mahjur) seizes another slave who is mahjur and the seized slave dies in the hand of the usurper, he guarantees it, because the mahjur is held accountable for his actions and this is among them, so he guarantees.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn followed by its expansion. The passage addresses a scenario where a legally incompetent slave (mahjur 'alayh) unlawfully seizes (ghasp) another such slave, and the seized slave dies while in the usurper's possession. The ruling holds the usurping slave liable (daman) for the value of the deceased slave. The reason given is that a mahjur, despite being under legal restriction due to minority or insanity, is nonetheless held accountable for his harmful acts (mu'ākhadh bi-af'ālihī). Consequently, he must compensate for the loss he caused, just as any other person would. This is a straightforward application of tort liability within the Hanafi school, where even those with limited legal capacity are responsible for damages they inflict.

Key Terms
محجور عليه (maḥjūr ʿalayh): A person who is legally restricted from managing their own property due to minority, insanity, or bankruptcy; here specifically a slave who is under such restriction.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage, here the obligation to pay the value of the deceased slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No liability because both are mahjur.
B) The usurper is liable and must compensate the value of the deceased slave.
C) The master of the usurper is liable for the value.
D) They share liability equally.

Key Takeaway
A mahjur (legally restricted) slave is liable for his tortious acts, including usurpation causing death, and must compensate the value.

8 min
Liability and Recoupment: The Mudabbar's Two Crimes
Detailed ruling on mudabbar slave's crimes at ghasp and master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the mudabbar crime scenario and explain the practical consequences of each position.

Core Idea
When a mudabbar commits two crimes, the master pays full value, but the debate is whether he can recoup the half paid to the first victim from the usurper a second time.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from Al-Bahr al-Raʼiq concerning a mudabbar slave who commits crimes in two different hands: first while usurped, then after return to the master. The master must pay the slave's value to the victims' heirs, but opinions differ on how the recoupment from the usurper should be handled. The disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad reflects deeper principles about the nature of compensation, causality, and the avoidance of combining substitute and substituted.

Guided Path
1
State the basic scenario
A mudabbar slave is usurped by a person (ghāṣib). While in the usurper's possession, the slave commits a crime (jīnāya). The usurper then returns the slave to the master. After return, the slave commits a second crime with the master. Identify who is liable and for what.
The mudabbar cannot be sold or handed over; his value (qīma) is paid.
2
Identify the initial ruling
The master must guarantee the full value of the mudabbar to the heirs of both victims, divided equally. Understand why the value is split: both crimes are equal in cause (sabab) because the slave's value is a single entity that cannot be split except proportionally.
If the second crime were not present, the first victim would get the entire value.
3
Locate the point of dispute
After the master pays the full value, he recovers half from the usurper (since one crime occurred at the usurper's). The question: should the master pay that half to the heir of the first crime? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say yes; Muhammad says no.
The basis of the dispute is whether paying it to the first victim leads to combining the substitute (money from the usurper) and the substituted (the slave's value) in the master's ownership.
4
Analyze the dalil for each position
Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf: The first victim had a right to the entire value originally; the competition from the second crime only reduced it. The master's recovery from the usurper is compensation for paying the first victim, so he should pass it on. Muhammad: Paying it to the first victim makes the master combine the substitute (the money he recovers) and the substituted (the value he already paid) in his ownership, which is impermissible, and also duplicates claims.
The Hanafi response to Muhammad uses an analogy: a dhimmi selling wine to pay a Muslim's debt – the dirhams are price in the dhimmi's perspective but substitute for the debt in the Muslim's perspective.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf, the master recovers the half from the usurper a second time after paying it to the first victim, so he ultimately recoups the full amount he paid for the first crime. Under Muhammad, the master does not pay the half to the first victim (he keeps it), so he does not recover a second time, and thus bears the loss of half the value of the first crime.
The practical difference: who bears the loss – the master (Muhammad) or the usurper (the two imams).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (مدبر جنى عند غاصبه ثم عند سيده ضمن قيمته لهما أي لو غصب رجل مدبراً فجنى عنده جناية ثم رده على مولاه فجنى عنده جناية أخرى ضمن المولى القيمة لولي الجنايتين فتكون بينهما نصفين لأن موجب جناية المدبر وإن كثرت قيمته واحدة فيجب ذلك على الملك للمولى لأنه هو الذي أعجز نفسه عن الدفع بالتدبير السابق من غير أن يكون مختاراً للفداء كما يصير في القن إذا أعتقه بعد الجنايات من غير أن يعلمها، وإنما كانت القيمة بينهما نصفين لاستوائهما في السبب. قال رحمه الله : ( ورجع بنصف قيمته على الغاصب أي رجع المولى بنصف ما ضمن من قيمة المدبر على الغاصب للتعدي لأنه ضمن القيمة بالجنايتين نصفها كان والنصف الآخر بسبب عنده فيرجع عليه لحقه من جهة الغاصب فصار كأنه لم يرد نصف بسبب يمتد العبد لأن رد المستحق بسبب وجد وعبده عند الغاصب كلا.رد قال رحمه الله : (ورده للأول) أي دفع المولى نصف القيمة الذي أخذه من الغاصب إلى ولي الجناية الأولى وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف . قالوا : ولهما أن حق الأول في جميع القيمة لأنه حين جنى في حقه لا يزاحمه أحد وإنما انتقص باعتبار مزاحمة الثاني إلى آخره. قال في العناية : واعترض بأن الثانية مقارنة للأولى حكماً فكيف يكون الحق للأول في جميع القيمة؟ وأجيب بأن المقارنة جعلت حكماً في حق الضمان لا غير والأولى مقدمة حقيقة وقد انعقدت موجبة لكل القيمة من غير مزاحمة وأمكن توفير موجبها فلا يمتنع بلا مانع أقول في الجواب بحث لأنا لا نسلم أن المقارنة جعلت حكماً في حق التضمين لا غير بل جعلت حكماً أيضاً في حق مشاركة ولي الجناية الثانية لولي الجناية الأولى كما أرشد إليه قول صاحب الهداية في الفصل السابق لأن الثانية مقارنة حكماً من وجه ولهذا يشارك ولي الجناية ا فإذا جعلت المقارنة حكماً في حق مشاركته وفي الجناية الثانية أيضاً كان ولي الجناية الثانية مزاحماً لولي الجناية الأولى في الاستحقاق جميع القيمة فكيف يأخذ ولي الجناية الأولى وحده كل القيمة مع مزاحمة الأولى الثانية له في استحقاقه إياه؟ وإن كان الاعتبار لتقدم الأولى حقيقة دون المقارنة الحكمية ينبغي أن لا يستحق ولي الثانية من قيمة المدبر وليس الأمر كذلك بالإجماع فليتأمل في جواب الشافعي. وقال محمد رحمه الله : لا يدفعها إليه لأن الذي يرجع به المولى على الغاصب عوض ما سلم لولي الجناية الأولى لأنه إنما يرجع على الغاصب فلا يدفع إليه كيلا يؤدي إلى اجتماع البدل والمبدل في ملك رجل، وكيلا يتكرر الاستحقاق. وقوله عوض ما سلم إلى ولي الجناية الأولى قلنا . كذلك هو شيئاً . قال : لكن ذلك في حق المولى والغاصب لأن ما أخذه المولى من الغاصب عوض المدفوع إلى ولي الجناية الأولى، وأما في حق المجني عليه فهو عوض ما لم يسلم له ومثله جائز كالذمي إذا باع خمراً وقضى دين مسلم يجوز له أخذه لأن تلك الدراهم ثمن الخمر في حق الذمي وبدل الدين في حق المسلم. قوله ودفع إلى الأول فإن قلت هذا يناقض قوله أولاً جناية العبد لا توجب إلا دفعاً واحداً لو محلاً أو قيمة واحدة وهنا أوجبت قيمة ونصفاً أو دفع العبد ونصف القيمة للأول، فالجواب أن الكلام الأول فيما إذا تعددت الجناية في يد شخص واحد من غير غصب ورد يكون جامعاً لها فلهذا تجب قيمة واحدة أو دفع واحد وهنا لما كانت عند شخصين لم يمكن جمعها فلها حكمان وإن كانت في يد واحد لكن بعد غصب ورد كما سيأتي في قوله «ورده». قال رحمه الله : (ثم يرجع به على الغاصب أي يرجع المولى بذلك الذي دفعه إلى ولي الجناية الأولى ثانياً على الغاصب عندهما لأنه استحق من يده بسبب كان في يد الغاصب فيرجع عليه بذلك فصار كأنه لم يرد ولم يضمن له شيئاً إذا لم يبق شيء من العبد أو من بدله في يده. قال رحمه الله : (وبعكسه لا يرجع به ثانياً أي بعكس ما ذكره لا يرجع غاصب المولى على الغاصب بالقيمة ثانياً وصورته أن المدبر جنى عند مولاه أولاً فغصبه رجل فجنى عنده جناية أخرى ثم رده على المولى ضمن قيمته لولي الجنايتين فيكون بينهما نصفين، ثم يرجع المولى على الغاصب بنصف القيمة لأنه استحق عليه بسبب كان في يد الغاصب فيدفعه إلى ولي الجناية الأولى بالإجماع. أما عندهما فظاهر، وأما عند محمد فإنه يمتنع الدفع إلي ولي الجناية الأولى في المسألة الأولى كيلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك واحد على ما بينا، وهنا لا يلزم ذلك لأن ما أخذه من ا الغصب عوض ما دفع إلى ولي الجناية الثانية فإذا دفعه إلى ولي الأولى لا يجتمع البدلان في ملك واحد، وفي الأول يجتمع لأنه عوض ما أخذه هو بنفسه، ثم إذا أدفعه إلى ولي الأولى لا يرجع به على الغاصب بالإجماع وهو المراد بقوله وبعكسه لا يرجع ثانياً» لأن المولى لما لم يدفع ما أخذه من الغاصب إلى ولي الأولى سلم له ما أخذه من الغاصب فلم يتصور الرجوع عليه وهنا لم يسلم له بالإجماع ومع هذا لا يرجع على الغاصب بالإجماع بما دفعه ثانياً لأن الذي دفعه المولى إلى ولي الجناية الأولى ثانياً هنا بسبب جناية وجدت عنده فلا يرجع به على أحد بخلاف المسألة الأولى عندهما لأن دفع المولى ثانياً إلى ولي الجناية الأولى فيها بسبب جناية وجدت عند الغاصب فيرجع عليه هنا كما ذكرنا . قال رحمه الله : ( والقن كالمدبر غير أن المولى يدلع العبد هنا وثمة القيمة) أي العبد القن فيما ذكرنا كالمدبر، ولا فرق بينهما إلا أن المولى يدفع القن وفي المدبر القيمة حتى إذا غصب رجل عبداً فجنى في يده ثم رده على المولى فجنى عنده جناية أخرى فإن المولى يدفعه إلى الأول ثم يرجع على الغاصب عندهما، وعند محمد لا يدفع ما أخذه من الغاصب إلى الأول بل يسلم له فلا يتصور الرجوع على الغاصب ثانياً على ما ذكرنا في المدبر، وإن جنى
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (A mudabbar committed a crime at his usurper's, then at his master's; he guarantees its value for both.) That is, if a man usurps a mudabbar, and he commits a crime with him, then returns him to his master, and he commits another crime with him, the master guarantees the value to the heirs of the two crimes, so it is divided equally between them, because the consequence of the mudabbar's crime, even if numerous, is one value, and that is due from the property to the master, because he is the one who rendered himself unable to hand over by the prior tadbir, without his being free to ransom, as happens in the case of a qinn if he manumits him after the crimes without knowing them. The value is divided equally because they are equal in cause. He said: (And he recovers half its value from the usurper.) That is, the master recovers half of what he guaranteed from the value of the mudabbar from the usurper due to transgression, because he guaranteed the value for the two crimes: half was due to the usurper's cause and the other half due to a cause with him, so he recovers against him for his right from the side of the usurper, so it is as if he did not return half because the return of the rightful claimant was due to a cause that existed, and his slave was with the usurper as if not returned. He said: (And he gives it to the first.) That is, the master pays the half of the value that he took from the usurper to the heir of the first crime. This according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. They said: The right of the first is in the entire value because when he committed the crime in his regard, no one competes with him; it was only reduced by the competition of the second, etc. In al-'Inaya it was objected: The second is legally simultaneous with the first, so how can the first have the right to the entire value? The answer was that simultaneity is only legally considered in the matter of liability, not otherwise, and the first is prior in reality and created a cause for the entire value without competition, and it is possible to fulfill its cause, so there is no prevention. I say in the answer there is discussion, because we do not concede that simultaneity is only considered in liability, but rather it is also considered in the participation of the heir of the second crime with the heir of the first, as the author of al-Hidayah indicated in the previous chapter, because the second is simultaneous in one respect, and so the heir of the second participates with the heir of the first in the claim to the entire value; how then can the heir of the first take the entire value alone with the competition of the second? If consideration is given to the priority of the first in reality without the legal simultaneity, then the heir of the second should not receive anything from the value of the mudabbar, but it is not so by consensus, so one should ponder the answer to al-Shafi'i. And Muhammad ibn al-Hasan said: He does not pay it to him, because what the master recovers from the usurper is compensation for what he surrendered to the heir of the first crime; he only recovers from the usurper, so he does not pay it to him, to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted in one person's ownership, and to avoid duplication of claims. His statement: compensation for what he surrendered to the heir of the first crime. We said: Similarly. He said: But that is regarding the master and the usurper, because what the master took from the usurper is compensation for what was paid to the heir of the first crime. As for the victim's right, it is compensation for what was not surrendered to him, and such is permissible, like the dhimmi who sells wine and pays a Muslim's debt; it is permissible for him to take it because those dirhams are the price of wine in the dhimmi's regard and the substitute for the debt in the Muslim's regard. His statement: and paid to the first. If you say this contradicts his earlier statement that the crime of a slave does not necessitate except one payment, either the person or one value, and here it necessitated a value and a half or payment of the slave and half the value to the first. The answer: The first speech is when the multiple crimes occur in one hand without usurpation and return, so only one value or one payment is due. Here, because they occurred with two persons, they cannot be combined, so they have two rulings, even if it was in one hand but after usurpation and return, as will come in his statement 'and he returned it'. He said: (Then he recovers it from the usurper.) That is, the master recovers that which he paid to the heir of the first crime a second time from the usurper according to the two, because he claimed a right from his hand due to a cause that was in the usurper's hand, so he recovers it from him, so it is as if he did not return and did not guarantee anything for him, since nothing remains of the slave or its substitute in his hand. He said: (And in the reverse case he does not recover it a second time.) That is, the opposite of what was mentioned: the master of the usurper does not recover the value from the usurper a second time. Its form: The mudabbar committed a crime with his master first, then a man usurped him, and he committed another crime with him, then returned him to the master; the master guarantees its value to the heirs of the two crimes, so it is divided equally between them. Then the master recovers half the value from the usurper because he has a claim against him due to a cause that was in the usurper's hand, and he pays it to the heir of the first crime by consensus. As for the two, it is apparent. As for Muhammad, he refrained from paying to the heir of the first crime in the first case to avoid combining substitute and substituted in one ownership, as we explained. Here it does not apply because what he took from the usurper is compensation for what was paid to the heir of the second crime; so if he pays it to the first, the two substitutes do not combine in one ownership. In the first case, they combine because it is compensation for what he himself took. Then if he pays it to the first, he does not recover it from the usurper by consensus, and that is what is meant by 'in the reverse case he does not recover a second time', because when the master did not pay what he took from the usurper to the first, he kept what he took from the usurper, so recovery is not conceivable. Here he did not keep it by consensus, and yet he does not recover from the usurper by consensus for what he paid a second time, because what the master paid to the heir of the first crime a second time here is due to a crime that occurred with him, so he does not recover it from anyone, unlike the first case according to the two, because the master's second payment to the heir of the first crime was due to a crime that occurred with the usurper, so he recovers it from him as we mentioned. He said: (And the qinn is like the mudabbar, except that the master hands over the slave here and there the value.) That is, the qinn slave in what we mentioned is like the mudabbar. The only difference is that the master hands over the qinn, and in the case of the mudabbar, the value. So if a man usurps a slave and he commits a crime in his hand, then returns him to his master, and he commits another crime with him, then the master hands him over to the first, then recovers from the usurper according to the two. And according to Muhammad, he does not pay what he took from the usurper to the first, but keeps it, so recovery against the usurper a second time is not conceivable, as we mentioned in the mudabbar.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on a ruling from the matn regarding the liability of a mudabbar slave (one whose manumission is deferred to after the master's death) who commits crimes in two different hands: first while in the possession of a usurper (ghāṣib), and then after being returned to his master. The commentary addresses how the compensation (qīma) is determined, distributed, and recouped, along with an intra-madhhab dispute between Imām Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf on one side, and Muḥammad ibn al-Ḥasan al-Shaybānī on the other. The core ruling: The master must pay the full value of the mudabbar slave (his market price) to the heirs of the two crime victims, divided equally, because the slave's value is a single entity that cannot be split between multiple crimes except proportionally. The master then recovers half of that value from the usurper (since one crime occurred at his hand), and pays that half to the heir of the first crime (the one that occurred at the usurper's). Then, according to Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf, the master recovers that same half again from the usurper, effectively getting full reimbursement for his payment to the first victim. Muḥammad disagrees, holding that the master should not pay the half he recovered to the first victim, to avoid combining the substitute (the money from the usurper) and the substituted (the slave's value) in the same ownership, and to prevent duplicate claims. The passage also discusses the reverse scenario (crime first at master, then at usurper), where the outcome differs. Finally, the rule for a regular slave (qinn) is parallel to that for a mudabbar, except that the master hands over the slave himself rather than its value. Throughout, the commentary uses legal reasoning (taʿlīl) based on causation, equality of cause, and juristic preference (istiḥsān) to derive and justify the rulings.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master has declared that he will be free upon the master's death (tadbīr); the master cannot sell him and the slave is protected from alienation.
قيمة (qīma): The market value of the mudabbar slave, which must be paid as compensation for crimes he commits that exceed his value.
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation, taking another's property without right; here, a person takes the mudabbar illegally and the slave commits a crime while in his possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, after the master pays the full value of the mudabbar for the two crimes, recovers half from the usurper, and pays that half to the first victim, what happens next?
A) The master recovers that half again from the usurper.
B) The master cannot recover anything further from the usurper.
C) The master gives the remaining half to the second victim.
D) The usurper pays the full value directly to the victims.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a mudabbar slave commits crimes in two different hands, the master pays full value, but the relied-upon position allows him to recoup the entire amount from the usurper for the first crime.

Liability for Death of a Usurped Free Child: Istihsan vs Qiyas
Ruling on liability when a free child dies after being usurped

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a usurped free child dies from location-specific causes, explain the istihsan argument, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the child as depositary.

Core Idea
A usurper's transgression in moving a free child to a dangerous place creates liability via istihsan, even though ghasb of a free person is not recognized.

This lesson examines a nuanced Hanafi ruling: a person who usurps a free child may be liable for the child's death if the cause is location-specific, like a snakebite. The school justifies this by istihsan, overriding the strict qiyas that would deny liability. We will trace the proof, the analogical reasoning, and the subsidiary dispute about a child as a depositary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Two Scenarios
The act is ghasb (usurpation) of a free child. The text distinguishes two outcomes: (1) sudden death or fever – no liability; (2) death from lightning or snakebite – diya on the usurper's 'aqila by istihsan.
Note that the ruling is about liability after death, not about the initial act of ghasb, which is haram.
2
Locate the Dalil: Istihsan and Qiyas
The text states that the apparent qiyas (analogy) would be no liability in both cases, which is the position of Zufar and al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi school adopts istihsan based on the concept of itlaf (destruction) by causation.
Istihsan here means preferring a stronger evidence over the apparent analogy.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah of Istihsan
The reasoning: moving the child to a place where such dangers exist is a transgressive act that destroys the protection (hifz) of the guardian. Since these dangers are not everywhere, the condition (shart) of moving is like the cause itself when there is transgression (ta'addi), analogous to digging a hole in a public path.
The key principle: 'شرط العلة بمنزلة العلة إذا كان تعدياً' (the condition of the cause is like the cause when there is transgression).
4
Note the Ikhtilaf: Zufar/Shafi'i and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Zufar and al-Shafi'i deny liability in all cases because ghasb of a free person is not recognized. Among Hanafis, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad distinguish between an entrusted slave (liable if killed by child) and food (not liable if eaten), while Abu Yusuf holds liability in both cases for the child as depositary.
The intra-Hanafi dispute extends to other scenarios like a child consuming entrusted wealth.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih
The text does not explicitly give a tarjih between the two approaches, but the main position presented (istihsan for the free child case) is the relied-upon (zahir al-riwayah). The deposit discussion is presented as a known difference, but the author leans towards Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's view.
The absence of an explicit tarjih indicates that the mufta bihi may vary; later Hanafi works generally follow Abu Hanifa in the deposit issue.

لأن عصمته لحق نفسه إذ هو مبقي على أصل الحرية في حق الدم فكانت عصمته لحق نفسه لا للمالك لأن عصمة المالك إنما تعتبر فيما له ولاية استهلاك حتى يمكن غيره من الاستهلاك بالتسليط وليس للمولى ولاية استهلاك عبده فلا يقدر أن يمكن غيره من ذلك فلا يعتبر تسليطه فيضمن الصبي باستهلاكه بخلاف سائر الأموال . قال في العناية : وإذا استهل الصبي ينظر؛ إن كان مأذوناً في التجارة وإن كان محجوراً عليه لكنه قبل الوديعة بإذن وليه ضمن بالإجماع إن كان محجوراً عليه وقبلها بغير أمر وليه فلا ضمان عليه عند الإمام ومحمد في الحال ولا بعد الإنزال، وقال أبو يوسف يضمن في الحال. وأجمعوا على أنه لو استهلك مال الغير من غير أن يكون عنده وديعة يضمن في الحال وهو تقسيم حسن ا هـ .
He usurped a free child and he died in his hand suddenly or from fever: he is not liable. If he died from a lightning bolt or snake bite, his blood money is upon the usurper's 'aqila. This is istihsan, and the qiyas is that he is not liable in both cases, and this is the saying of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on them, because ghasb (usurpation) of a free person is not realized. Do you not see that it is not realized for a mukatab even if he is a minor because he is free in hand despite being a slave in neck? So a free person in hand and neck is more deserving that he is not made liable by it. The face of istihsan is that this is a guarantee of destruction (damān itlāf), not a guarantee of ghasb, and the child is liable for destruction. This is because moving him to a land of predators or to a place of lightning is destruction from him by causation, and he is transgressing by removing the hand of the protector, who is the master, so he is liable. This is because snakes, predators, and lightnings are not in every place, so it is possible to protect him with him. When he moved him while he is transgressing, he has removed the master's protection from him transgressingly, so it is attributed to him because the condition of the cause is like the cause when there is transgression, like digging in the road. Unlike death suddenly or from fever, because that does not vary by place, unless he moved him to a place where fevers and diseases prevail, then he is liable, and the blood money is obligatory upon the 'aqila because it is a killing by causation. Unlike the mukatab because he is in his own hand, and even if he is a minor, he is attached to the major. Do you not see that he is not married except with his consent, like an adult, while the minor free person is married by his guardian without his consent. So if he took him out of the master's hand and he died from something that can be guarded against, he is liable. The mukatab is not incapable of protecting himself, so he is not liable by ghasb, like a major free person. If he could not protect himself, then he is not liable by ghasb, but if he made something like shackles and the like, the mukatab is liable, and also like a major free person, just as the minor is liable, because then the destruction is attributed to the usurper due to negligence in protection. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: Like a child who was entrusted with a slave and killed it, and if he was entrusted with food and ate it, he is not liable. That is, the usurper's 'aqila is liable just as the child's 'aqila is liable if he killed a slave that was entrusted with him. This distinction between the entrusted slave and the entrusted food is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on them, said: The entrusted child is liable in both cases. On this basis, if the child who is interdicted was entrusted with wealth and consumed it, he is not taken for guarantee immediately according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, but is taken after emancipation. According to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on them, he is taken immediately. On this same difference is the acknowledgement concerning the slave and the executor, and likewise lending in them. Then Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, required in al-Jami' that the child be rational. And in al-Jami' al-Kabir: The issue is placed concerning a child whose age is twelve years, and that indicates that the non-rational is liable by agreement. And because empowerment is not considered for him, while his action is considered. For Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on them, if he destroys property that is valuable, protected, and a right of the owner, then guarantee is obligatory upon him, just as if the trust was a slave, or if the child was permitted to trade and to protect by the guardian, and as if he destroyed something other than what was in his hand. And it was not immune because of the established authority of consumption in it. And for both of them, he destroyed property that is not taken for its guarantee, as if he destroyed it with his permission and approval. This is because immunity (isma) is established as a right for him, and he has forfeited it upon himself where he placed it in a non-preventing hand, so it does not remain immune unless he appointed another in his place for protection, and there is no appointment here because he has no authority over the child to make him liable, and the child has no authority over himself to undertake liability, unlike the permitted one because he has authority over himself like an adult. And unlike if the trust was a slave, because his immunity is for his own right, as he is remaining on original freedom regarding blood, so his immunity is for his own right, not for the owner, because the owner's immunity is considered only in what he has authority to consume so that he can authorize others to consume by empowerment. The master has no authority to consume his slave, so he cannot empower others to do that, so his empowerment is not considered, and the child is liable by consuming it, unlike other properties. He said in al-Inaya: When the child is entrusted, it is considered: if he is permitted to trade, or if he is interdicted but accepted the trust with the permission of his guardian, he is liable by consensus. If he is interdicted and accepted it without the order of his guardian, then there is no guarantee upon him according to Imam and Muhammad immediately nor after the reduction. Abu Yusuf said: He is liable immediately. And they are unanimous that if he consumed the property of another without having a trust with him, he is liable immediately. And this is a good division. End.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its proof and intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the liability of a person who usurps a free child (ghasb al-sabi al-hurr). The base ruling states that if the child dies suddenly or from fever in the usurper's possession, there is no liability. However, if the child dies from a lightning strike or snakebite, the blood money (diya) is due on the usurper's 'aqila (male paternal relatives who pay blood money). The author explains that this is istihsan (juristic preference), while the qiyas (analogy) would be no liability in either case, as held by Zufar and al-Shafi'i. The reason for the distinction is that usurpation of a free person is not legally recognized in the same way as a slave; the free child remains a free agent. However, istihsan applies because the usurper's act of moving the child to a place where such dangers exist is a form of destruction by causation (itlaf bi al-tasabbub). Since the usurper removed the child from his guardian's protection, and such dangers are not ubiquitous, the child could have been protected at home. Thus, the liability is akin to digging a hole in a public path. In contrast, sudden death or fever are not place-specific, so no liability attaches unless the usurper moved the child to a place where such diseases are prevalent. The text then distinguishes the case of a child who was entrusted with a slave and kills it vs. entrusted with food and eats it. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the child's 'aqila is liable for the slave (because the slave's immunity is inherent, not due to the owner's permission), but not for the food (because food is consumable by nature). Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold liability in both cases. The discussion extends to other scenarios like a child entrusted with wealth and the differences in immediate vs. deferred liability. The passage concludes with a quote from al-Inaya categorizing the child's liability based on whether he is permitted to trade or interdicted, and whether he accepted the trust with guardian's permission.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property or person by force without right; here applied to a free child.
دية (diya): Blood money: financial compensation paid to the heirs of a homicide victim; here on the 'aqila for killing by causation.
عاقلة ('aqila): The male paternal relatives of the killer who are liable to pay diya in cases of accidental killing.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: abandoning the apparent qiyas for a stronger evidence or equity; here overrides the qiyas of non-liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position (istihsan), what is the ruling if a usurper takes a free child and the child dies from a snakebite?
A) The usurper is not liable because the child is free.
B) The usurper's 'aqila must pay diya.
C) The usurper must pay a fixed penalty of half the diya.
D) The child's parents are liable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school imposes diya on the usurper's 'aqila for a free child's death from location-specific dangers based on istihsan, treating the transgressive act as causation even though ghasb of a free person is not recognized.

Definition and Introduction to Qasamah
Definition and scenario of qasamah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qasamah linguistically and legally, and identify the key elements (pillar, condition, description) as outlined in the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Qasamah is a set of oaths taken by the residents where a slain body is found, swearing innocence.

This passage opens the chapter on qasamah, explaining its placement at the end of the book of diyat (blood money). The author outlines six aspects of discussion: linguistic meaning, legal meaning, pillar, condition, description, and proof. The focus here is on the definition and the scenario that triggers qasamah according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six aspects of qasamah
The author enumerates six aspects: linguistic meaning, legal meaning, pillar, condition, description, and proof. Recognize that this passage covers the first two and partially the sixth.
This is a standard analytical framework in Hanafi fiqh texts.
2
Distinguish linguistic vs. legal meaning
Linguistically, qasamah is a noun meaning 'oaths' (aqsam). Legally (shari'ah), it is defined as oaths taken by residents of a locality where a slain person with traces of violence is found.
The legal definition adds the specific context and procedure.
3
Extract the Hanafi position from the narration
The passage quotes Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa: qasamah applies when a slain person is found in a city locality or someone's house with a wound, beating mark, or strangulation, and the killer is unknown.
This defines the scenario (sabab) that triggers qasamah.
4
Identify the conditions (shurut) implied
From the narration, conditions include: (1) presence of visible traces of violence, (2) the killer is unknown, (3) the body is found within a defined dwelling area (mahalla or dar).
These conditions are prerequisites for the qasamah procedure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القسامة لما كان أمر القتيل يؤول إلى القسامة فيما إذا لم يعلم قاتله ذكرها هنا في باب على حدة في آخر الديات والكلام في القسامة من وجوه الأول في معناها لغة. والثاني في معناها شرعاً. والثالث في ركنها والرابع في شرطها والخامس في صفتها. والسادس في دليلها. اعلم أن القسامة في اللغة اسم وضع موضع الأقسام؛ كذا في عامة الشروح المغرب. وقال في معراج الدراية القسامة لغة مصدر أقسم كما لا يخفى على من بعلم الأدب. وأما في علم الشريعة فهي أيمان يقسم بها أهل محلة أو دار أو غير ذلك وجد فيها قتيل به أثر يقول كل منهم والله ما قتلته ولا علمت له قاتلاً؛ كذا في العناية. قال في النهاية : وأما تفسيرها شرعاً فما روى أبو يوسف عن أبي حنيفة أنه قال في القتيل الذي يوجد المحلة أو دار رجل في المصر إن كان جراحة أو أثر ضرب أو أثر خنق ولا يعلم قاتله له دراية
Chapter of Qasamah. Because the matter of the slain person leads to qasamah when his killer is not known, he mentioned it here in a separate chapter at the end of the book of Diyat. And the discussion of qasamah is from aspects: first, its meaning in language; second, its meaning in shari'ah; third, its pillar; fourth, its condition; fifth, its description; sixth, its proof. Know that qasamah in language is a noun placed in the position of oaths; thus in most of the commentaries (shuruh) and al-Mughrib. And it is said in Miraj al-Diraya: Qasamah linguistically is a verbal noun from 'aqsama' (to swear), as is not hidden from one who knows literature. As for in the science of shari'ah, it is oaths by which the people of a locality (mahalla) or house or other place where a slain person is found with traces swear, each of them saying: 'By Allah, I did not kill him, nor do I know a killer for him.' Thus in al-Inaya. It is said in al-Nihaya: As for its interpretation in shari'ah, it is what Abu Yusuf narrated from Abu Hanifa that he said concerning the slain person who is found in a locality or a man's house in the city: if there is a wound or mark of beating or mark of strangulation, and his killer is not known, he has insight (or: he is responsible?)
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on qasamah (oaths in homicide). The author explains why this chapter is placed at the end of the book of diyat: when a person is found killed and the killer is unknown, the matter leads to qasamah. He outlines six aspects of discussion: linguistic meaning, legal meaning, pillar, condition, description, and proof. The linguistic meaning is that it is a noun for oaths (aqsam). The legal meaning is that qasamah consists of oaths taken by the residents of a locality or house where a slain body is found bearing signs of violence. Each resident swears: 'By Allah, I did not kill him nor do I know his killer.' The passage then quotes from al-Nihaya a narration from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa defining the scenario: a slain person found in a city locality or someone's house with injuries like a wound, beating, or strangulation, and the killer is unknown. The text ends abruptly with 'he has insight' (lahu dirayah), possibly incomplete.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasamah): A set of oaths taken by the residents of a locality where a slain person is found, each swearing they did not kill him or know the killer.
محلة (mahalla): A locality or quarter within a city where the slain person is found.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what triggers the procedure of qasamah?
A) A slain person is found with traces of violence and the killer is known.
B) A slain person is found with traces of violence and the killer is unknown.
C) A slain person is found without any traces.
D) A person is accused of murder by the victim's family.

Key Takeaway
Qasamah is a collective oath of innocence sworn by the residents where a slain person with traces is found and the killer is unknown.

The 'Aqila: Hanafi Definition and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Defines 'Aqila and presents Hanafi position on diwan liability with Shafi'i objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'Aqila, explain why the Hanafi school holds that the diwan (military register) can be liable for diyah, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The 'Aqila is defined by mutual support, not blood alone—Hanafis include the diwan because it is the source of support.

This passage from *al-Bahr al-Ra'iq* presents the core Hanafi ruling on who constitutes the 'Aqila—the group liable for blood money (diyah) in unintentional killings. It then contrasts the Shafi'i position and defends the Hanafi view with proofs from the Sunnah and the practice of Caliph 'Umar. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi jurists adapt rulings to social structures while preserving the underlying rationale of alleviating the killer's burden.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly who the Hanafi school considers as 'Aqila: for those registered in the diwan, the diwan is liable; for others, the agnatic relatives ('asaba).
Note the distinction: diwan liability is specific to Hanafi.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State Imam al-Shafi'i's position: the 'Aqila are always the agnatic relatives by lineage, never the diwan.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i rationale: liability follows lineage (as in inheritance and maintenance) and the original rule from the Prophet's time was on the 'asaba; there is no evidence to change it.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: Umar's establishment of diwan liability was not a repeal but a confirmation of the underlying principle of mutual support (tanasur). The means of support changed from tribe to diwan, so the liability shifts accordingly.
Key concept: taqrir al-ma'na (confirming the meaning) rather than naskh (repeal).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: in a state with a diwan system, diyah is taken from the diwan stipends over three years; otherwise from the 'asaba. Shafi'i always takes from 'asaba.
This difference can affect who ultimately pays the blood money.

Book of 'Aqila. His 'Aqila are the people of the diwan who have provision in the treasury and whose names are written in the diwan. Whoever has no diwan is from the agnatic relatives, not from the people of the diwan. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him: the 'aql is on his agnatic relatives from lineage, not on the people of the diwan. Al-Tahawi from our companions mentioned that it is obligatory in the wealth of the killer because the obligation of 'aql on the 'Aqila is custom contrary to analogy, because holding non-offender liable for the offender is repugnant to analogy. The Law only obligated it on the people of the diwan or on the tribe, so what is besides them remains on the rule of analogy. Whoever has neither diwan nor tribe: it is said consideration is given to residence and help of hearts, so the closest [view]; and it is said it is obligatory in his wealth; and it is said it is obligatory in the treasury wealth; similarly the foundling is on this disagreement. A city does not bear responsibility for another city, but a city bears responsibility for its villages because 'aql is built on mutual support and cooperation. The people of each city rely on the diwan of their city and do not rely on the diwan of another city. The people of each city rely on their rural areas and villages even if their dwellings are far, because the desert is one desert, so they are like the people of the diwan in one city, they cooperate with the people of the city even if their dwellings are far. If two deserts differ, they are like two cities. The 'Aqila of the manumitter is the tribe of his freed slave, and the freed slave of wala' bears responsibility for his freed slave and his tribe. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: Every diyah that is obligated by the killing itself is on the 'Aqila. The 'Aqila are the group who bear the 'aql, which is the diyah. It is said: I wadaitu the killed person if I gave his diyah, and I 'aqaltu about the killer, i.e., I paid on his behalf what was due of diyah. We have mentioned the diyah and its types in the Book of Diyat. As for its obligation on the 'Aqila, the basis for it is what is authentically reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he ruled for the diyah of a woman who was killed and the diyah of her fetus on the 'asaba of the 'Aqila. The father of the killer against whom the judgment was given said: O Messenger of Allah, how can I pay for one who neither cried nor drank nor ate, and such is lost? He said, peace be upon him: This is from soothsaying. And because the soul is sacred, so there is no way to disregard it, and there is no obligation on the mistaken one because he is excused, so error is lifted from him. Obligating all of it on him is a punishment because of its severity and destruction, so the 'Aqila are joined to him to achieve alleviation, so they are more deserving of being joined. His saying 'Every diyah that is obligated by the killing itself' excludes what becomes wealth by settlement or by doubt, because the enemy (?) obligates punishment so he does not deserve alleviation, so the 'Aqila do not bear it for him. In the Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam, some criticized and said: There is no injury from the 'Aqila and the obligation of diyah is due to it, so it is on the wealth of the killer. His statement 'And no bearer of burdens bears the burden of another' (Quran 6:164) supports that. Do you not see that whoever destroys an animal is liable for it from his wealth? So likewise the obligation of diyah. We say: The obligation of diyah on the 'Aqila is well-known, established by well-known hadiths, and upon it is the practice of the Companions and those after them, and it is not contradicted by the Book of Allah.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about who constitutes the 'Aqila (the group liable for blood money). The Hanafi ruling states that the 'Aqila are either the people of the diwan (military register) if the killer is registered, or the agnatic relatives ('asaba) if he is not. Imam al-Shafi'i, on the other hand, holds that the 'Aqila are always the agnatic relatives by lineage, regardless of the diwan. The Hanafi school bases its position on the practice of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, who established the diwan system and placed diyah liability on the diwan members, with the approval of the Companions. The text also discusses the underlying rationale: imposing diyah on the 'Aqila is a concession (rukhsa) that goes against the general principle (qiyas) that only the offender should be liable. Islamic law made an exception for the 'Aqila to provide relief to the unintentional killer, as obligating the full diyah on him would be oppressive. Therefore, the exception is limited to what the texts specify. The passage also addresses subsidiary issues: if the killer has no diwan or tribe, some scholars refer to his residence and helpers; others say it is on his own wealth or the public treasury. Further, a city does not bear for another city, but a city bears for its villages. The 'Aqila of a manumitter is the tribe of the freed slave. The proof for the Hanafi position is the authentic hadith about the woman and her fetus, where the Prophet placed the diyah on the 'asaba of the 'Aqila, showing that liability is on those who support the killer, not necessarily blood relatives. The main proof is Umar's rulings in the presence of the Sahaba without objection, which constitutes a consensus (ijma'). The Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument is that this practice is not a repeal (naskh) but a confirmation of the underlying meaning (taqrir al-ma'na), because the means of support changed from tribal alliances to the diwan system in Umar's time.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'Aqila): The group of people who are liable to pay the diyah (blood money) on behalf of the unintentional killer.
الديوان (al-diwan): The military register in which names of soldiers and their stipends are recorded.
العشيرة (al-'ashira): The clan or extended family; synonym for 'asaba in this context.
التناصر (al-tanasur): Mutual support and cooperation, the underlying rationale for 'Aqila liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary basis for determining who constitutes the 'Aqila?
A) Lineage and blood relation
B) Mutual support and cooperation (tanasur)
C) The killer's personal wealth
D) The nearest city

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi 'Aqila is defined by the source of mutual support, which can be either the diwan (for registered soldiers) or the agnatic relatives, whereas the Shafi'i school restricts 'Aqila to blood lineage.

The Hanafi Ruling on Diyah Collection from the Diwan
Rules on diyah collection from diwan with evidence and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah collection from the diwan over three years and explain the evidence from the Prophet and Umar.

Core Idea
Diyah is collected from the diwan's stipends over three years as a concession, based on reports from the Prophet and Umar's practice.

This lesson explains the Hanafi position on how diyah is collected when the killer belongs to the diwan (military register). The school holds that it is taken from the stipends over three years, a concession to alleviate burden. The text presents the evidence and addresses a dispute with al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the collection of diyah from the diwan's stipends. The ruling is wajib, based on the principle of alleviation.
The diwan refers to the military registry.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is the reported practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the implementation by Umar with Companion consensus.
The text references 'mawri' and 'mahki'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The specification of three years indicates a concession contrary to analogy, so it is confined to what is reported.
The underlying principle is alleviation, not analogy.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Al-Shafi'i holds that diyah is on the 'asaba (tribe), based on earlier reports and analogy to inheritance.
The dispute is about the locus of liability.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi reliance on Umar's practice with no objection from the Companions gives it a stronger basis as a development of the meaning (support).
This is not a repeal but a reapplication.

He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And they are the people of the diwan if the killer is from among them; it is taken from their stipends in three years. The people of the diwan are the army whose names are written in the diwan, and this is our position. Al-Shafi'i said: On the people of the tribe, as we have narrated, and it was like that until the time of 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, and there is no repeal after the Prophet, peace be upon him, so it remains as it was, and because it is a connection, and relatives are more deserving of it like inheritance and maintenance. Our proof is the rulings of 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, for when he organized the diwans, he placed the diyah on the people of the diwan in the presence of the Companions without any objection from them. That is not a repeal but a confirmation of the meaning, because it was originally on the people of support, and they used to support each other through alliances, clientage, and enmity, but in the time of 'Umar, support came through the diwan, so he placed it on its people following the meaning. They said: If today they support each other through a craft, then their 'Aqila would be the people of that craft; if through a group, then its people. And the diyah is a connection as he said, but imposing it on one's trade goods is more appropriate than imposing it on his capital, because he is more entitled, and the 'Aqila only assumed liability for alleviation. The estimation of three years is reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, and narrated from 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, end. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If the stipends come out in more years or less, it is taken from them to achieve the intended goal, because the goal is alleviation and it has been achieved. Three? I say: there is a discussion, that analogy would reject imposing wealth in exchange for a sacred soul due to lack of equivalence, except that the Law has come with that as they stated. The Law only came with imposing it deferred for three years, for it is reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, and narrated from 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, as previously. So the deferment should be restricted to three years, since it is established with them that the ruling that comes contrary to analogy is confined to what is mentioned. A similar example will come in the book regarding the explanation that what is obligated on the killer from his wealth, like when a father kills his son intentionally, is not immediate in our school but deferred for three years; consider if it can be refuted. This is if the stipends are for future years, such that if they accrued in past years before the judgment of diyah and then came out after judgment, nothing is taken from them because the obligation is by judgment. If the stipends of three future years come out in one year, all the diyah is taken from them because it is after the obligation, as the obligation is by judgment, and the intended alleviation has been achieved. If the obligatory amount is one third of the diyah or less, it is due in one year; if more, it is due in two years up to two-thirds; then if more than that up to the full diyah, it is due in three years, because the collection of the diyah is over three years, so each third is necessarily in one year. The obligation on the killer is like the obligation on the 'Aqila, so it is due in three years; that is like the father who kills his son intentionally when retaliation is converted to property. If ten persons kill one person by mistake, then on the 'Aqila of each of them is one-tenth of the diyah in three years, considering the part as the whole, and it is the substitute for the soul, so each part is deferred for three years. The beginning of the period is considered from the time of judgment of the diyah, because the original obligation is the diyah and its conversion to value. Allah: (And if there is no diwan, then on his 'Aqila) as we have narrated at that time. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it is divided among them in three years; not more than one dirham or one and one-third dirham is taken from each person in each year, and not exceeding from each person from the whole diyah in
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage details the practical ruling on how diyah is collected from the 'Aqila, specifically when the killer belongs to the diwan (military register). The Hanafi school holds that the diyah is taken from the stipends of the diwan members over three years. This is based on reports from the Prophet and the practice of 'Umar. The text addresses several scenarios: if the stipends are paid over more or fewer than three years, the diyah is adjusted accordingly to achieve the goal of alleviation. If the stipends of three future years come out in one year, all the diyah is taken at once because the obligation is by judicial judgment and the purpose is served. The amount taken per person per year is limited: not more than one dirham or one and one-third dirham, and the total taken from one person over three years does not exceed four dirhams (the text is cut off but it implies a maximum). The ruling also applies to the killer himself in certain cases, like a father killing his son intentionally when retaliation is converted to diyah; even then, it is deferred over three years. If multiple killers are involved, each killer's 'Aqila bears a proportional share, also deferred over three years. The period starts from the time of judicial judgment, not from the killing, because the obligation is established by judgment. The underlying principle is that this is a concession contrary to analogy, so it is strictly limited to what the texts specify. The text also mentions that if today people support each other through a craft or profession, then the 'Aqila would be the people of that craft, illustrating that the basis is mutual support, not fixed blood relations.

Key Terms
العطايا (al-'ataya): The stipends paid to soldiers registered in the diwan.
التأجيل (al-ta'jil): The deferment of diyah payment over three years for alleviation.
القضاء (al-qada'): The judicial judgment that establishes the obligation of diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, diyah from the diwan is taken from:
A) stipends over three years
B) killer's personal wealth immediately
C) 'asaba over three years
D) diwan in one year

Key Takeaway
Diyah from the diwan is a wajib concession collected from stipends over three years, starting from the judicial judgment.
